The Three Types of Middle Term in Compound Syllogisms
Author enumerates three cases for the middle term in compound syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the middle term in a compound syllogism from a conditional and a disjunctive as complete, incomplete, or mixed in the premises.

Core Idea
The middle term's completeness determines the structure of compound syllogisms.

In the fifth type of compound syllogisms, which combines conditional and disjunctive premises, the key is the middle term (al-awsat). The author divides this into three cases based on whether the middle term is a complete proposition in both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. Understanding these three cases is essential for analyzing such syllogisms before applying conclusion rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
In a compound syllogism from conditionals and disjunctives, we have two premises. One is a conditional (muttasila) and the other is a disjunctive (munfasila). The middle term (al-awsat) is the part that is common between them.
2
Check if the middle term is a complete proposition
Determine if the middle term appears as a full proposition (a complete conditional or disjunctive) or as a part of a proposition (like the antecedent or consequent). The author uses 'complete part' (juz' tamm) and 'incomplete part' (ghayr tamm).
3
Apply the three-case classification
For each premise, note whether the middle term is a complete part or not. Then classify as: (a) complete in both, (b) incomplete in both, or (c) complete in one and incomplete in the other.
This classification is purely structural; the conclusion rules depend on this but are covered separately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من الأقسام الخمسة فيما يتألف من المتصلة والمنفصلة وأنه على ثلاثة أقسام : فإن الأوسط فيه (أ) إما أن يكون جزءا تاما من كل واحدة من المقدمتين، (ب) أو غير تام من كل واحدة منهما، (ج) أو تاما في إحدى المقدمتين غير تام من الأخرى. ... وكذلك إذا كان إحدى المقدمتين جزئية لكن النتيجة جزئية مانعة الخلو لما أن امتناع اجتماع الشيء مع اللازم مما يوجب امتناع اجتماعه مع الملزوم هذا إذا كانت المقدمتان موجبتين. فأما إذا كانت إحداهما سالبة... وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية فإنه ينتج سالبة جزئية مانعة الخلو إن كانت الجزئية منفصلة لما مر في بيان المنفصلة الكلية المانعة من الخلو ... وكذلك إذا كانت إحدى المقدمتين جزئية لكن النتيجة جزئية لما مر . وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الجمع فيما يكون كل واحد من مقدمتين كلية فإنه ينتج متصلة موجبة جزئية من عين الأصغر ونقيض الأكبر... وكذلك إذا كانت إحداهما جزئية لما مر . وأما إذا كانت إحداهما سالبة فالسالبة إن كانت مانعة الجمع تنتج سالبة مانعة الجمع... وكذلك إذا كانت إحداهما جزئية لكن النتيجة جزئية، وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو فلا تنتج أصلا...
The fifth division of the five divisions of what is composed of the conditional and the disjunctive, and it is of three types: for the middle term in it is either (a) a complete part from each of the two premises, (b) or an incomplete part from each of them, (c) or complete in one premise and incomplete in the other. ... And likewise if one of the two premises is particular, the conclusion is a particular exclusive-disjunctive because the impossibility of the coexistence of a thing with the necessary entails the impossibility of its coexistence with the necessitating, this when both premises are affirmative. As for if one of them is negative... And if one of the two premises is particular, it yields a particular negative exclusive-disjunctive if the particular is disjunctive according to what passed in the explanation of the universal exclusive-disjunctive... And likewise if one of the two premises is particular but the conclusion is particular according to what passed. And if the disjunctive is preventive-of-conjunction in the case where each of the two premises is universal, it yields a particular affirmative conditional from the same minor and the contradictory of the major... And likewise if one of them is particular according to what passed. And if one of them is negative, then the negative if it is preventive-of-conjunction yields a negative preventive-of-conjunction... And likewise if one of them is particular but the conclusion is particular. And if the disjunctive is exclusive-disjunctive, it yields nothing at all...
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion from al-Mantiq al-Kabir on the fifth type of compound syllogisms formed from conditional and disjunctive premises. The author first enumerates three cases for the middle term: complete part of both premises, incomplete part of both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other. Then, he examines the conclusions under various conditions, considering whether the disjunctive is preventive-of-conjunction (man'at al-jam') or exclusive-disjunctive (man'at al-khuluww), and the quantity and quality of the premises. For instance, when both premises are affirmative and one is particular, the conclusion is a particular exclusive-disjunctive, because if A entails B, then the impossibility of A and B together implies impossibility of A and the entailing condition. The passage also covers cases with negative premises and the different types of disjunctives, showing that some combinations yield no conclusion. The discussion presupposes familiarity with prior rules for conditionals and disjunctives.

Key Terms
الأوسط
متصلات
منفصلات

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following best describes the three cases for the middle term in a compound syllogism from conditionals and disjunctives?
A) Complete in both premises, incomplete in both, or complete in one and incomplete in the other.
B) Universal in both, particular in both, or universal in one and particular in the other.
C) Affirmative in both, negative in both, or affirmative in one and negative in the other.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
The three-case classification of the middle term is the foundational step for analyzing compound syllogisms from conditionals and disjunctives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given: Premise 1 (conditional): 'If the act is obligatory (A), then reward is expected (B)'. Premise 2 (disjunctive): 'Either the act is obligatory (A) or it is forbidden (C)'. Identify the middle term and classify its completeness in each premise.
?
Construct a compound syllogism where the middle term is incomplete in both premises, using an Islamic example from fiqh (e.g., regarding rulings on transactions).

Conclusion Rules for Compound Syllogisms from Conditionals and Disjunctives
Author provides rules for conclusions based on disjunctive type and premise features

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct conclusion of a compound syllogism from a conditional and a disjunctive, given the type of disjunctive and the quality and quantity of premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion type depends on whether the disjunctive is man'at al-jam' or man'at al-khuluww.

After understanding the three structures of the middle term, the author proceeds to explain how to derive conclusions. These rules vary based on whether the disjunctive is preventive-of-conjunction (man'at al-jam') or exclusive-disjunctive (man'at al-khuluww), and whether the premises are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. This lesson will guide you through these rules using the passage's detailed cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive premise
Determine if the disjunctive is man'at al-jam' (prevents coexistence) or man'at al-khuluww (prevents both being false). This is crucial for the conclusion.
2
Check the quantity and quality of the premises
Note if each premise is universal or particular, affirmative or negative. The author specifies cases for universal/particular and affirmative/negative combinations.
3
Apply the rule from the passage
For each combination, the author gives the conclusion. For example, when both premises are affirmative and universal, and the disjunctive is man'at al-khuluww, the conclusion is a particular exclusive-disjunctive. When one premise is negative, different rules apply.
4
Verify using the underlying logical principle
The author often justifies by referring to earlier discussions about the relationship between a thing and its necessary condition (for man'at al-khuluww) or the contradictory of the major (for man'at al-jam').

Why Conditional Syllogism is Prior to Categorical
Author argues that conditional syllogism is prior to categorical in rank and power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that categorical syllogisms depend on conditional syllogisms for their validity.

Core Idea
The categorical syllogism proves nothing without the conditional 'If...then'.

The author presents an objection that the conditional syllogism is more fundamental than the categorical. Even categorical syllogisms, when scrutinized, rely on a conditional inference. This lesson unpacks that argument, showing how the definition of qiyas leads to the priority of the conditional form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector claims that the conditional syllogism (istithna'i) is prior in rank and power to the categorical conjunctive syllogism (iqtirani hamli). Find this claim in the Arabic.
The claim is that the very definition of qiyas necessitates this priority.
2
Understand the definition of qiyas
The objector points out that the definition of qiyas states that the syllogism itself is the ground (malzum) for the conclusion. This means the syllogism necessitates the conclusion.
Malzum here refers to the entire syllogism, not just a premise.
3
Extract the hidden conditional in categorical syllogisms
The objector argues that someone using a categorical syllogism implicitly says: 'If this categorical syllogism is true, then the conclusion is true.' This is a conditional premise.
The categorical syllogism alone is not sufficient; it requires this conditional to yield the conclusion.
4
Recognize the conditional syllogism
The statement 'If the categorical is true, then the conclusion is true; but the categorical is true; so the conclusion is true' is itself a conditional syllogism (modus ponens). Thus, the categorical syllogism is only effective when coupled with this conditional form.
This shows that categorical syllogisms are essentially a special case of conditional syllogisms.
5
Conclude the priority
Since the categorical syllogism cannot produce its conclusion without the conditional syllogism, the conditional is prior in both rank (rutba) and power (quwwa).
Rank refers to logical fundamentality; power refers to epistemic force.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول: إن الحد الذي ذكرتموه للقياس يقتضي أن يكون القياس الاستثنائي مقدما في الرتبة على الاقتراني الحملي ؛ وذلك لأنكم سلمتم أن الملزوم للنتيجة هو القياس، فالمتمسك بالقياس الحملي كان يقول: إن كان هذا القياس الحملي حقا كانت النتيجة حقا، لكن هذا القياس الحملي حق، فالنتيجة حقة. فثبت أن القياس الاقتراني الحملي، لا ينتج إلا بقوة القياس الاستثنائي، فوجب أن يكون القياس الاستثنائي مقدما فى الرتبة والقوة على الحملي.
And a questioner may say: The definition you mentioned for the syllogism necessitates that the conditional syllogism be prior in rank to the categorical conjunctive syllogism; that is because you conceded that the ground for the conclusion is the syllogism, so the one adhering to the categorical syllogism would say: If this categorical syllogism is true, then the conclusion is true; but this categorical syllogism is true, so the conclusion is true. Thus it is established that the categorical conjunctive syllogism does not produce a conclusion except by the power of the conditional syllogism. So it is necessary that the conditional syllogism be prior in rank and power to the categorical.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and response from Sharh 'Uyun al-Hikmah, attributed to al-Mantiq al-Kabir, concerning the priority of the conditional syllogism (al-qiyas al-istithna'i) over the categorical conjunctive syllogism (al-iqtirani al-hamli). The objector argues that the definition of qiyas implies that the conditional syllogism is more fundamental. The reasoning is that even a categorical syllogism ultimately relies on a conditional form: one must say 'If this categorical syllogism is true, then its conclusion is true; but it is true, so the conclusion is true.' This is itself a conditional syllogism (modus ponens). Therefore, the validity of the categorical syllogism depends on the conditional syllogism. Hence, the conditional syllogism is prior in both rank (al-rutba) and power (al-quwwa). This passage highlights the foundational role of conditional reasoning in the entire syllogistic system.

Key Terms
قياس استثنائي
قياس اقتراني حملي
الملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Because conditional syllogisms were historically discovered first.

Key Takeaway
The categorical syllogism is only valid when subsumed under a conditional syllogism, making the conditional syllogism more fundamental in rank and power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a simple categorical syllogism from kalam: 'Everything originated has a cause; the world is originated; therefore the world has a cause.' Rewrite it as a conditional syllogism (modus ponens) and identify the conditional premise.
?

Rules for Disjunctive Syllogisms with Particular Premises
Author enumerates rules for disjunctive syllogisms when one premise is particular, covering exclusive and inclusive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid conclusion for a disjunctive syllogism when one premise is particular, for both exclusive-of-void and exclusive-of-conjunction types.

Core Idea
When one premise is particular, the conclusion is always particular; the type of disjunction determines validity.

We have already seen how universal disjunctive syllogisms work. But what happens when one premise is particular? The author now expands the rules, showing that the conclusion is always particular, and that the type of disjunction—exclusive-of-void or exclusive-of-conjunction—dictates whether a valid syllogism is produced at all. Understanding these rules prevents false inferences in theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Classify the disjunction as exclusive-of-void or exclusive-of-conjunction
Determine whether the disjunction is māni'at al-khulū (inclusive: at least one true) or māni'at al-jam' (exclusive: at most one true). This is the foundational distinction because the rules differ significantly.
Check the wording of the disjunction: 'either-or' can be ambiguous in natural language.
2
Assess the quantity and quality of the premises
The passage deals with cases where at least one premise is particular. Also note whether any premise is negative, as that affects the rule for exclusive-of-void.
If both premises are universal, refer to the earlier universal rules.
3
Apply the rule for exclusive-of-void (māni'at al-khulū) with a particular premise
If the particular premise is affirmative, the conclusion is a particular negative proposition. If a premise is negative, no conclusion follows at all.
Example: 'Some A are B or C; this particular A is B; therefore some A are not C?' – Actually, the rule says particular negative conclusion. More precisely: from 'Some S are P or Q' and 'This S is not P', conclude 'This S is Q'? But the passage specifies 'particular negative' as the conclusion type.
4
Apply the rule for exclusive-of-conjunction (māni'at al-jam') with a particular premise
When both premises are universal, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional (muttaṣila) from the minor term and the contradictory of the major. If one premise is particular, the conclusion is also particular. If a premise is negative, the conclusion is negative exclusive-of-conjunction.
The conclusion here is a conditional proposition, not a categorical.
5
Verify the conclusion's quantity and type
Ensure the conclusion is particular and matches the expected quality (negative or affirmative) and proposition type (categorical or conditional) as per the rule applied.
If the disjunction is exclusive-of-void and a premise is negative, remember that no conclusion is produced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إحدى المقدمتين جزئية فإنه ينتج سالبة جزئية مانعة الخلو إن كانت الجزئية منفصلة لما مرَّ في بيان المنفصلة الكلية المانعة من الخلو ... وكذلك إذا كانتْ إحدى المقدمتين جزئية لكنَّ النتيجة جزئية لما مرَّ . وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الجمع فيما يكون كل واحد من مقدمتين كلَّيَّة فإنه ينتج متّصلة موجبة جزئية منْ عين الأصغر ونقيض الأكبر... وكذلك إذا كانتْ إحداهما جزئية لما مر . وأمَّا إذا كانت إحداهما سالبة فالسالبة إن كانت مانعة الجمع تنتج سالبة مانعة الجمع... وكذلك إذا كانتْ إحداهما جزئيَّة لكنَّ النتيجة جزئية، وإن كانت المنفصلة مانعة الخلو فلا تنتج أصلا... ].
If one of the two premises is particular, then it produces a particular negative [syllogism] of the exclusive type if the particular [premise] is a disjunction, as previously mentioned in the explanation of the universal exclusive disjunction... And likewise if one of the two premises is particular, but the conclusion is particular, as previously mentioned. And if the disjunction is of the exclusive-of-conjunction type, when each of the two premises is universal, then it produces a particular affirmative conjunctive [syllogism] from the minor term itself and the contradictory of the major... And likewise if one of them is particular, as previously mentioned. As for if one of them is negative, then if the negative is of the exclusive-of-conjunction type, it produces a negative exclusive-of-conjunction [syllogism]... And likewise if one of them is particular, but the conclusion is particular. And if the disjunction is of the exclusive-of-void type, it produces nothing at all...].
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of disjunctive syllogisms (qiyās munfaṣil) by examining cases where one premise is particular. The author first considers the exclusive-of-void type (māni'at al-khulū), where the disjunction is inclusive (at least one must be true, both may be true). If one premise is particular, the conclusion is a particular negative. For the exclusive-of-conjunction type (māni'at al-jam'), where both cannot be true, if both premises are universal, the conclusion is a particular affirmative conjunctive (muttaṣila) using the minor term and the contradictory of the major. If one premise is particular, the conclusion is also particular. When one premise is negative, if it is exclusive-of-conjunction, the conclusion is also negative exclusive-of-conjunction. Finally, for exclusive-of-void type, if one premise is particular, no conclusion is produced. These rules mirror those for universal premises but with particular conclusions.

Key Terms
منفصلة
مانعة الخلو
مانعة الجمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a disjunctive syllogism of the exclusive-of-void type (māni'at al-khulū) where one premise is particular, what is the conclusion?
A) A universal affirmative
B) A particular negative
C) No conclusion
D) A particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
In disjunctive syllogisms with one particular premise, the conclusion is always particular, and the type of disjunction (exclusive-of-void vs exclusive-of-conjunction) determines whether a conclusion follows and its quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid disjunctive syllogism of the exclusive-of-conjunction type with one particular premise on a kalam topic (e.g., attributes of Allah). State the premises and conclusion, and explain which rule applies.
?
Identify which rule applies to the following argument: 'Either some accidents are eternal or they are originated. But they are not eternal. Therefore, some accidents are originated.' Determine the type of disjunction and whether the conclusion follows.

The Priority of the Exceptional Syllogism: A Dialectical Objection
Objection on priority of exceptional syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objection that the exceptional syllogism is prior to the conjunctive categorical syllogism and analyze its logical structure using the definition of qiyas.

Core Idea
The exceptional syllogism is the hidden engine that drives every conjunctive categorical syllogism.

This passage presents a dialectical objection challenging the standard ordering of syllogisms. The objector argues that the very definition of qiyas (syllogism) requires the exceptional syllogism (istithnā'ī) to be ranked higher than the conjunctive categorical (iqtirānī ḥamlī). The reasoning is that the conjunctive syllogism only yields its conclusion when embedded within a conditional structure. Understanding this objection deepens our appreciation of how syllogisms function and reveals the foundational role of conditional reasoning in logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Find the main assertion: the exceptional syllogism is prior in rank and power to the conjunctive categorical syllogism.
The claim is based on the definition of qiyas.
2
Extract the premises of the objection
The objector uses two premises: (1) the definition of qiyas makes the syllogism the necessary condition (malzūm) for the conclusion; (2) the conjunctive categorical syllogism only produces its conclusion when cast as an exceptional syllogism (if-then).
3
Reconstruct the embedded conditional syllogism
The defender of the categorical syllogism implicitly uses: 'If this categorical syllogism is true, then the conclusion is true; but this categorical syllogism is true; therefore the conclusion is true.' This is an exceptional syllogism (modus ponens).
Thus the categorical syllogism depends on the exceptional form.
4
Analyze the force of the objection
The objector concludes that the exceptional syllogism is more fundamental. Consider whether this objection is valid or whether it can be answered (the author refers to a fuller discussion elsewhere).
This step requires critical thinking about the relationship between the two syllogism types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرح عيون الحكمة في موضوع "القياس"، يقول: [ولقائل أن يقول: إنَّ الحد الذي ذكرتموه للقياس يقتضي أن يكون القياس الاستثنائي مقدما في الرتبة على الاقتراني الحملي ؛ وذلك لأنكم سلمتم أنَّ الملزوم للنتيجة هو القياس، فالمتمسك بالقياس الحملي كان يقول: إن كان هذا القياس الحملي حقا كانت النتيجة حقا، لكنَّ هذا القياس الحملي حقٌّ، فالنتيجة حقة. فثبت أنَّ القياس الاقتراني الحملي، لا ينتج إلا بقوَّة القياس الاستثنائي، فوجب أن يكون القياس الاستثنائي مقدَّما فى الرتبة والقوَّة على الحملي. فهذه إشارة إلى بعض مباحث هذه المسألة والاستقصاء فيها مذكور فى المنطق الكبير ](١). وقد بحث هذه المسألة في المنطق الكبير (و ١٥٠ و١٥١و، ١٥٨و، ١٥٩ و)
Sharḥ 'Uyūn al-Ḥikma on the subject of 'qiyas' says: [For one may say: Indeed the definition you have given for qiyas requires that the exceptional syllogism be prior in rank to the conjunctive categorical syllogism; and that is because you have conceded that the necessary condition for the conclusion is the [entire] syllogism. For the one adhering to the categorical syllogism would say: If this categorical syllogism is true, then the conclusion is true; but this categorical syllogism is true, so the conclusion is true. Thus it is established that the conjunctive categorical syllogism only produces [its result] by virtue of the exceptional syllogism. Therefore it is necessary that the exceptional syllogism be prior in rank and power to the categorical. This is an indication to some investigations of this question, and the full inquiry is mentioned in the Mantiq Kabir](1). And he discussed this question in Mantiq Kabir (folios 150-151, 158, 159).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical objection from the commentary on 'Uyūn al-Ḥikma. The objector argues that the definition of qiyas (syllogism) logically requires that the exceptional syllogism (istithnā'ī) be considered prior to the conjunctive categorical (iqtirānī ḥamlī). The reasoning is that the conjunctive syllogism, to be valid, must be embedded in an exceptional syllogism: one says, 'If this categorical syllogism is true, then the conclusion is true; but this categorical syllogism is true, therefore the conclusion is true.' Thus the exceptional form is the underlying structure. Therefore, the objector claims, the exceptional syllogism holds a higher rank and power. The author notes that this issue is discussed fully in the larger work Mantiq Kabir, implying a more detailed response is given there.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
Parts and Types of Syllogism
Author defines parts of syllogism and divides it into conjunctive and exceptional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the parts of a syllogism (premises and terms) and classify a syllogism as conjunctive or exceptional based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated in the premises.

Core Idea
Every syllogism has premises and terms; their roles distinguish conjunctive from exceptional syllogisms.

This passage lays the groundwork for all syllogistic reasoning by dissecting its components. We will learn what premises and terms are, and then see how syllogisms are divided into two families based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated. This understanding is essential for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ingredients
Recognize that syllogisms are composed of propositions called premises, and each premise is composed of terms (subject and predicate).
2
Analyze a premise
Understand that after removing the quantifier, modality, and copula from a categorical premise, only the subject and predicate remain as essential parts (terms).
The quantifier (sūr) and modality (jiha) are not essential; the copula indicates connection but disappears after analysis.
3
Observe an example
Work through the categorical syllogism example: 'Every body is composite, every composite is temporally originated, therefore every body is temporally originated.' Identify that 'every body is composite' and 'every composite is temporally originated' are the premises, and 'body', 'composite', 'temporally originated' are the terms.
The entire structure is the syllogism; the consequent is the conclusion when the syllogism is performed.
4
Distinguish by conclusion presence
Classify based on whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) is explicitly stated in the premises. If not stated explicitly (only potential), it is a conjunctive syllogism. If stated explicitly, it is an exceptional syllogism.
The author prefers 'exceptional' over 'conditional' because some conditional syllogisms are conjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما القضايا التي يتركب منها القياس فإنَّها تسمى مقدمات وهي أجزاء القياس لا محالة. وأما أجزاء المقدّمة وهي التي تبقى بعد التحليل فإنَّها تسمى حدودًا. فالمقدمة إذا كانت حمليّة تبقى بعد التحليل إلى آخر أجزائها الذاتية الموضوع والمحمول ليس إلا، إذ السُّور والجهة ليستا من الأجزاء الذاتية لها، والرابطة وإن كانت ذاتيَّة لكنَّها تدلُّ على الارتباط ولا مجال للارتباط بعد التحليل. ولنمثل للقياس والمقدّمة والحدود مثالاً وهو كلُّ جسم مؤلف، وكلُّ مؤلف محدث فكل جسم محدث فقولنا كل جسم مؤلَّف مقدِّمة وكذلك كلُّ مؤلَّف محدث مقدّمة أخرى. وأجزاؤهما من الجسم والمؤلف والمحدث حدود، ومجموع المقدمتين على النظم الذي مرَّ ذكره قياس، واللازم عنه وهو أنَّ كل محدث فذلك عند اللزوم نتيجة وقبل اللُّزوم عند استعمال الذهن بترتيب القياس وإقامته عليه مطلوب. وكما أنَّ القياس ينقسم بحسب المادة إلى الإقامة التي مرَّ ذكرها فكذلك بحسب الصورة ينقسم إلى ما يكون من الأقيسة الاقترانية، وإلى ما يكون من الأقيسة الاستثنائية؛ وذلك لأنَّ اللَّازم عن القياس وهو النتيجة أو نقيضها. وبالجملة أحد طرفي المطلوب لا يخلو من أن يكون مذكورًا في القياس بالفعل أي بالتصريح أو لا يكون. (أ) فإن لم يكن مذكورًا فيه بالفعل بل بالقوة فذلك القياس يسمى قياسًا اقترانياً مثل قولنا كل جسم مؤلّف وكلُّ مؤلّف كما مرَّ ؛ فإن اللازم وهو كل جسم محدث لم يكن مصرَّحًا به بالفعل ولا نقيضه لكنَّه بالقوة؛ لما أنه تحت المؤلف وقد صرح فيه بأنَّ كلَّ مؤلف محدث . (ب) وإن كان مذكورًا فيه بالفعل إمّا اللازم أو نقيضه فذلك القياس يسمى قياسًا استثنائياً. والجمهور يسمونه قياسًا شرطيا. غير أنه لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ من الشرطيات ما يكون على سبيل الاقتران فالأولى أنه يسمى بالاستثنائي. وذلك مثل أن يقال لو كان هذا جسما لكان جوهرا لكنّه جسم فيلزم أن يكون جوهرًا، أو يقال لو كان هذا جسما لكان جوهرًا لكنّه
As for the propositions from which the syllogism is composed, they are called premises, and they are necessarily parts of the syllogism. As for the parts of the premise, which remain after analysis, they are called terms. So when the premise is categorical, after analysis only the subject and predicate remain as its essential parts, because the quantifier and modality are not among its essential parts, and the copula, although essential, indicates connection, and there is no room for connection after analysis. Let us give an example of the syllogism, premise, and terms: 'Every body is composite, and every composite is temporally originated, so every body is temporally originated.' Our statement 'Every body is composite' is a premise, and likewise 'Every composite is temporally originated' is another premise. Their parts—body, composite, and temporally originated—are terms. The entirety of the two premises in the order mentioned is a syllogism. The necessary consequent thereof, which is 'Everything is temporally originated'—that at the time of necessity is the conclusion, and before necessity, when the mind is employed in arranging the syllogism and performing it, is the sought proposition. Just as the syllogism is divided according to matter into the demonstrations mentioned earlier, so too according to form it is divided into conjunctive syllogisms and exceptional syllogisms. That is because the consequent of the syllogism—which is the conclusion or its contradictory—in summary, one of the two sides of the sought proposition is either mentioned in the syllogism actually (i.e., explicitly) or not. (A) If it is not mentioned in it actually but only potentially, then that syllogism is called a conjunctive syllogism, like our statement 'Every body is composite, and every composite...' as previously; the consequent—'Every body is temporally originated'—was not explicitly stated, nor its contradictory, but it is potential, because it is under 'composite' and it was explicitly stated that 'every composite is temporally originated.' (B) And if it is mentioned in it actually, either the consequent or its contradictory, then that syllogism is called an exceptional syllogism. The general run [of scholars] call it a conditional syllogism, but it is not as it should be, since there are conditionals that are conjunctive. So it is better that it be called 'exceptional.' An example is: 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is a body, so it follows that it is a substance,' or 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is...'
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines the fundamental parts of a syllogism. It begins by stating that the propositions composing a syllogism are called premises (muqaddamāt), which are the essential parts. The components of a premise after analysis are called terms (ḥudūd). Using a categorical premise as an example, the author explains that after removing the quantifier (sūr), modality (jiha), and copula (rābiṭa), only the subject and predicate remain as the essential parts. An example is given: 'Every body is composite, every composite is temporally originated, therefore every body is temporally originated.' Here, 'every body is composite' and 'every composite is temporally originated' are the premises; 'body,' 'composite,' and 'temporally originated' are the terms; and the entire structure is the syllogism. The consequent is the conclusion (natīja) when the syllogism is performed. The passage then divides the syllogism by form into two types: conjunctive (iqtirānī) and exceptional (istithnā'ī). The distinction is based on whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) is explicitly stated in the premises. If it is not explicit but only potential, the syllogism is conjunctive; the example given is the one above, where 'every body is temporally originated' is not stated directly but follows from the premises. If the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated, the syllogism is exceptional. The author notes that many scholars call the latter 'conditional' (sharṭī), but that is imprecise because some conditional syllogisms are conjunctive; 'exceptional' is more accurate. Two examples of exceptional syllogisms are partially given: 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is a body, therefore it is a substance,' and a second example that is cut off.

Key Terms
مقدمة
حد
قياس اقتراني
قياس استثنائي

A syllogism's structure consists of premises and terms; its classification as conjunctive or exceptional depends on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated in the premises.

The Syllogism

Dividing Syllogisms by Form: Iqtirani and Istithna'i
Author divides syllogisms by form into conjunctive and exceptive, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a conjunctive (iqtirani) and an exceptive (istithna'i) syllogism based on whether the conclusion is explicitly stated, and identify which type a given example belongs to.

Core Idea
The key difference between the two types is whether the conclusion is stated explicitly or only implied.

This passage classifies syllogisms by their form, complementing the earlier material division. The author introduces a simple but powerful criterion: whether the conclusion (or its contradictory) is explicitly mentioned in the premises. Understanding this distinction helps you recognize the logical structure of arguments in Islamic sciences like fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
The author states that a syllogism is divided by form based on whether the necessary consequent (the conclusion or its contradictory) is mentioned explicitly (bil-fi'l) or only potentially (bil-quwwa) in the premises.
The criterion is not about the content but about the expression of the conclusion.
2
Contrast the two types
If the conclusion is not mentioned explicitly, it is iqtirani (conjunctive). If it is mentioned (either the conclusion or its contradictory), it is istithna'i (exceptive).
Iqtirani corresponds to categorical syllogisms; istithna'i corresponds to hypothetical syllogisms with a categorical premise.
3
Analyze the author's examples
The iqtirani example: 'Every body is composed, everything composed is created, so every body is created' — the conclusion is not stated but implied. The istithna'i example: 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is a body, so it is a substance' — the conclusion is explicitly stated.
In istithna'i, the conditional premise is explicit and a categorical premise either affirms the antecedent or denies the consequent.
4
Understand the terminology correction
The author notes that many call istithna'i 'sharti' (conditional), but that is imprecise because conditionals can also be conjunctive. He insists on 'istithna'i' to avoid ambiguity.
This shows attention to precision in logical terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما أنَّ القياس ينقسم بحسب المادة إلى الإقامة التي مرَّ ذكرها فكذلك بحسب الصورة ينقسم إلى ما يكون من الأقيسة الاقترانية، وإلى ما يكون من الأقيسة الاستثنائية؛ وذلك لأنَّ اللَّازم عن القياس وهو النتيجة أو نقيضها. وبالجملة أحد طرفي المطلوب لا يخلو من أن يكون مذكورًا في القياس بالفعل أي بالتصريح أو لا يكون. (أ) فإن لم يكن مذكورًا فيه بالفعل بل بالقوة فذلك القياس يسمى قياسًا اقترانياً مثل قولنا كل جسم مؤلّف وكلُّ مؤلّف كما مرَّ ؛ فإن اللازم وهو كل جسم محدث لم يكن مصرَّحًا به بالفعل ولا نقيضه لكنَّه بالقوة؛ لما أنه تحت المؤلف وقد صرح فيه بأنَّ كلَّ مؤلف محدث . (ب) وإن كان مذكورًا فيه بالفعل إمّا اللازم أو نقيضه فذلك القياس يسمى قياسًا استثنائياً. والجمهور يسمونه قياسًا شرطيا. غير أنه لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ من الشرطيات ما يكون على سبيل الاقتران فالأولى أنه يسمى بالاستثنائي. وذلك مثل أن يقال لو كان هذا جسما لكان جوهرا لكنّه جسم فيلزم أن يكون جوهرًا، أو يقال لو كان هذا جسما لكان جوهرًا لكنّه ليس بجوهر فيلزم أن لا يكون جسما.
Just as the syllogism is divided according to matter into the demonstrations mentioned, so too according to form it is divided into conjunctive syllogisms and exceptive syllogisms. This is because the necessary consequent of the syllogism, which is the conclusion or its contradictory, and in general one of the two sides of the desired, is either explicitly mentioned in the syllogism (i.e., stated) or not. (a) If it is not explicitly mentioned but potentially, then that syllogism is called a conjunctive syllogism, like our saying 'Every body is composed, and everything composed...' as before: the necessary consequent, which is 'every body is temporally originated', was not explicitly stated, nor its contradictory, but it is potential because it is under 'composed' and it was stated that 'everything composed is temporally originated.' (b) If it is explicitly mentioned, either the consequent or its contradictory, then that syllogism is called an exceptive syllogism. The majority call it a conditional syllogism, but that is not appropriate, because among conditionals there are conjunctive ones, so it is better to call it exceptive. An example is: 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is a body, so it follows that it is a substance,' or 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is not a substance, so it follows that it is not a body.'
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage classifies syllogisms by form. It parallels the material division (burhan, jadal, etc.) with a formal division into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i). The criterion is whether the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated. In iqtirani, it is only potential; in istithna'i, it is explicit. An example of iqtirani is the previous 'every body is composed, everything composed is temporally originated' — the conclusion is not stated but implied. An example of istithna'i is a conditional with a categorical premise affirming or denying the antecedent or consequent: 'If this were a body, it would be a substance; but it is a body, so it follows that it is a substance.' The author notes that the common term 'sharti' (conditional) is not ideal because conditionals can also be conjunctive. Thus the preferred term is 'istithna'i'.

Key Terms
قياس اقتراني
قياس استثنائي
لازم
نقيض
بالفعل
بالقوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Some animals are not rational.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism is iqtirani if the conclusion is only implicit, and istithna'i if the conclusion or its contradictory is explicitly stated.

The Six Types of Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates six types of iqtirani syllogisms based on premises composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given iqtirani syllogism into one of six types based on its premises.

Core Idea
Conjunctive syllogisms are classified by premise composition: categorical or conditional.

Now that we understand the basics of conjunctive syllogisms, we must learn how to distinguish between different types. The author provides a clear classification: six types determined by whether the premises are categorical or conditional (connected or disjunctive). This taxonomy is essential for analyzing the structure of any compound argument in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises of the syllogism
Look at both premises. Determine if each is a categorical proposition (simple subject-predicate), a connected conditional (if...then), or a disjunctive conditional (either...or).
2
Count how many of each type
Note the combination: two categoricals, two connected, two disjunctive, or mixed pairs.
3
Match to one of the six types
The six types are: (1) both categorical, (2) both connected, (3) both disjunctive, (4) categorical + connected, (5) categorical + disjunctive, (6) connected + disjunctive.
Mixed types can be in any order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن نشتغل ببيان الاقترانيات؛ إذ الاقترانيَّات بالتقديم أولى لما أنها قد تكون من حمليات ساذجة والحمليّات من القضايا مقدمة على الغير فنقول: القياس الاقترانية بحسب ما يتركَّب من الحمليَّات والشَّرطيَّات إلى ستة أقسام؛ وذلك لأنَّه (أ) إمَّا أنْ يتركَّب من حمليتين (ب) أو من متصلتين (ج) أو من منفصلتين، أو من مختلفتين : وذلك (د) من حملي ومتصل (هـ) أو من حملي ومنفصل (و) أو من متصل ومنفصل. وستعرف كل قسم من هذه الأقسام بأمثلتها الظاهرة.
Now we occupy ourselves with explaining conjunctive syllogisms, because conjunctive syllogisms are given priority since they may consist of simple categoricals, and categorical propositions are given precedence over others. So we say: Conjunctive syllogisms, according to what they are composed of from categoricals and conditionals, are of six types: (a) composed of two categoricals, (b) two connected conditionals, (c) two disjunctive conditionals, or of mixed types: (d) a categorical and a connected conditional, (e) a categorical and a disjunctive conditional, and (f) a connected and a disjunctive conditional. Each type will be known by its clear examples.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage classifies iqtirani syllogisms into six types based on the nature of the premises: all categorical, all connected conditionals, all disjunctive conditionals, and then mixed pairs. The author gives priority to categorical-iqtirani because categorical propositions are foundational. This classification is foundational for studying the formal structure of syllogisms. The six types are: (1) two categoricals, (2) two muttasila (connected conditionals), (3) two munfasila (disjunctive conditionals), (4) one categorical and one muttasila, (5) one categorical and one munfasila, (6) one muttasila and one munfasila.

Key Takeaway
Conjunctive syllogisms are of six types based on whether the premises are categorical or conditional (connected or disjunctive).

Objection to Self-Productivity of First Figure Moods
Author presents an objection against self-productivity of first figure moods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate objections to the self-productivity of first figure syllogisms using conditional reasoning.

Core Idea
Even seemingly self-evident logical moods require justification; objections expose hidden assumptions.

In this lesson, we examine a sophisticated objection against the claim that the productive moods of the first figure are productive by themselves (lidhatihi). The objector presents three arguments aimed at showing that the conclusion does not follow from the premises alone. This challenge forces us to clarify what it means for a syllogism to be 'productive by itself' and prepares the ground for deeper defense of the first figure's validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target claim
The objection targets the claim that the four productive moods of the first figure are productive by themselves. 'By itself' means the conclusion follows necessarily from the premises alone, without external help. What does this entail?
2
Examine the first objection: equality argument
The objector argues: if the conclusion is equal to the premises, it cannot be a consequent (lazim) because a consequent must be distinct; if not equal, there is no necessary connection. Trace this reasoning and identify the two conditional statements.
3
Examine the second objection: dependence on other moods
The objector says if the conclusion follows from something else (like another mood), then it is not by itself; but if not, it also follows from the second mood. This appears circular. Analyze the structure: is this a valid dilemma?
4
Analyze the third objection (incomplete)
The third argument deals with the subject and predicate in the conclusion, though it is cut off. Even so, we can see it challenges the necessity of the connection. Speculate on what the full argument might be.
5
Evaluate the overall objection
Does this objection succeed? Consider possible responses: does the objector confuse 'following from premises' with 'being identical to premises'? Is the second argument question-begging? Formulate a defense of self-productivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول: إنَّ المنتج في هذا الشكل من الضُّروب الأربعة لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإن من الوجوه ما ينافيه نحو الوجوه المذكورة في الشكل الأول وغيرها. وذلك من وجوه أيضًا: (۱) منها أن يقال هذا اللازم لا يلزم عنه لذاته فإنَّه إذا لزم فلا يخلو من أن يكون مساويا له : وإنَّه باطل ضرورة كونه لازما، واللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره. أو لا يكون مساويًا، وإنَّه باطل أيضًا ضرورة عدم الاقتران بينهما أصلا وإلا لا يكون لازما أبدًا. (۲) ومنها أن يقال إنَّه لا يلزم عنه لذاته فإنه إذا لزم: فإما أن يلزم من الغير وإنَّه محال فإنه يفتقر وجوده إلى وجود أحد ملزوميه حينئذ؛ وذلك هو الغير. ولو كان مفتقرًا إلى الغير فلا يلزم عنه لذاته. وإما أن لا يلزم من الغير، و إنَّه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزم من الضرب الثاني في الشكل الأول: وذلك غيره. (۳) ومنها أن يقال إنَّه لا يلزم عنه لذاته؛ إذ الموضوع في النتيجة وهي قولنا لا شيء من «ج» «أ» لا يخلو من أن يكون غير المحمول أو لا يكون، وإنما قد لا
Then one might object: The productive moods of this figure, among the four, are not productive by themselves, because there are aspects that contradict it, like the aspects mentioned in the first figure and others. This is also from several aspects: (1) One may say: This necessary consequent does not follow from it by itself, because if it follows, then it is either equal to it or not. If it is equal, that is necessarily false because it is a consequent, and a consequent of something else can exist when that other exists without the existence of another. If it is not equal, that is also false because there is no conjunction between them at all, otherwise it would never be a consequent. (2) One may say: It does not follow by itself because if it follows, then either it follows from something else or not. If it follows from something else, that is impossible because its existence would then depend on the existence of one of its two malzumati [things that entail it]; that other is the something else. If it depended on something else, it would not follow by itself. If it does not follow from something else, that is also impossible because it follows from the second mood of the first figure — that is something else. (3) One may say: It does not follow by itself because the subject in the conclusion, which is our statement 'No J is A,' is either different from the predicate or not... but the text cuts off.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the claim that the productive moods of the first figure are productive by themselves (lidhatihi). The objector gives three arguments: (1) The conclusion (lazim) is either equal to the premises or not. If equal, it is impossible because a consequent must be distinct; if not equal, there is no necessary connection between premises and conclusion. (2) The conclusion either follows from something else or not. If from something else, then it depends on that, so it is not by itself; if not from something else, that is also impossible because it does follow from the second mood. (3) The third point, though incomplete, deals with the subject-predicate relation in the conclusion. This is a typical dialectical objection in logic texts, probing the foundation of syllogistic figures. The author likely proceeds to refute it, but the passage is cut off.

Key Terms
منتج (muntij): Productive (of a mood): that which yields a necessary conclusion. [Note: No significant shift — standard term for a valid mood.]
لازم (lazim): The consequent; here the conclusion of a syllogism. [Note: No significant shift — standard.]
مساو (musawi): Equal; in the objection, it refers to the conclusion being identical to the premises. [Note: No significant shift — standard.]
لذاته (lidhatihi): By itself; i.e., the conclusion follows from the premises alone without external factors. [Note: No significant shift — standard logical notion.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objector, what is the problem with the conclusion being equal to the premises?
A) It would mean the conclusion is a consequent, which is impossible because a consequent must be distinct.
B) It would mean the premises are false.
C) It would make the syllogism invalid.
D) It would require a middle term.

Key Takeaway
Objections to self-productivity force a deeper understanding of what it means for a conclusion to follow necessarily from premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the first objection (equality argument) and formalize it as a conditional syllogism (if...then). Identify the antecedent, consequent, and the form (modus tollens, modus ponens, etc.) used by the objector. Then assess its validity using the rules of istithna'i syllogisms.
?
Find a real argument from Usul al-Fiqh that uses a first figure syllogism (e.g., 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden'). Apply the same objection from this lesson to that argument. Does the objection hold? Write a brief defense.

Six Types of Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author classifies qiyas iqtirani into six types based on proposition combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the six types of conjunctive syllogisms based on the composition of their premises and identify the proposition types involved.

Core Idea
The conjunctive syllogism splits into six types by mixing categorical and conditional premises.

In this passage, the author introduces a systematic classification of conjunctive syllogisms. Understanding these six types is crucial because each type has its own rules of validity in later sections. Let's walk through the classification step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three basic proposition types
Recognize the three kinds of propositions that can serve as premises: hamli (categorical), muttasil (conjunctive conditional), and munfasil (disjunctive conditional).
These are the building blocks of the classification.
2
Enumerate the pure combinations
When both premises are of the same type, we get three possibilities: two hamlis, two muttasils, or two munfasils. These correspond to the first three divisions (a, b, c) in the author's list.
3
Enumerate the mixed combinations
When the two premises are of different types, we get three more possibilities: hamli+muttasil, hamli+munfasil, and muttasil+munfasil. These are the remaining three divisions (d, e, f).
4
Memorize the six divisions using the author's labeling
The author uses letters أ, ب, etc. as labels. Associate each letter with the corresponding combination. For example, (أ) is two hamlis, (ب) is two muttasils, etc.
This labeling helps in later referencing.
5
Connect to upcoming examples
The author promises to clarify each division with clear examples. Be prepared to match each type to a concrete syllogism in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والآن نشتغل ببيان الاقترانيات؛ إذ الاقترانيَّات بالتقديم أولى لما أنها قد تكون من حمليات ساذجة والحمليّات من القضايا مقدمة على الغير فنقول: القياس الاقترانية بحسب ما يتركَّب من الحمليَّات والشَّرطيَّات إلى ستة أقسام؛ وذلك لأنَّه (أ) إمَّا أنْ يتركَّب من حمليتين (ب) أو من متصلتين (ج) أو من منفصلتين، أو من مختلفتين : وذلك (د) من حملي ومتصل (هـ) أو من حملي ومنفصل (و) أو من متصل ومنفصل. وستعرف كل قسم من هذه الأقسام بأمثلتها الظاهرة: هذا بحسب ما يتركَّب منه .
And now we occupy ourselves with explaining the conjunctive [syllogisms]; for conjunctive [syllogisms] are given priority because they may consist of simple subject-predicate propositions, and subject-predicate propositions are given precedence over others. So we say: The conjunctive syllogism, according to what it is composed of from subject-predicate and conditional propositions, is of six divisions: because it is either (a) composed of two subject-predicate [propositions], (b) or two conjunctive conditionals, (c) or two disjunctive conditionals, or of two different ones: that is (d) a subject-predicate and a conjunctive, (e) or a subject-predicate and a disjunctive, (f) or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. And you will know each of these divisions through their clear examples: this is according to what it is composed of.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a classification of categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) into six types. The author begins by explaining why they start with conjunctive syllogisms: they are given priority because they may be composed of simple subject-predicate (hamli) propositions, which themselves are given precedence over other types of propositions. Then the author presents the classification: a conjunctive syllogism is composed of two propositions. These two propositions can both be subject-predicate (hamliyya), both be conjunctive conditionals (shartiyya muttasila), both be disjunctive conditionals (shartiyya munfasila), or one of each: hamli and conjunctive, hamli and disjunctive, or conjunctive and disjunctive. This makes six divisions. The author uses Arabic letters (أ, ب, etc.) as labels for the cases, though these are not always needed in commentary. The student should memorize these six combinations as they form the basis for understanding the moods of categorical syllogisms. The author promises to clarify each with examples later.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني
حملي
متصل
منفصل

Checkpoint

D) 5

Key Takeaway
The conjunctive syllogism is divided into six types based on whether its two premises are categorical, conjunctive conditional, or disjunctive conditional.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

First Objection to First Figure Productivity: The Coextensive Consequence Dilemma
Author presents first objection and begins answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first objection against the first figure's necessity of consequence and evaluate the author's answer using the sunrise-daylight example.

Core Idea
Constant conjunction does not prevent logical consequence; coextensive consequences are possible.

Logicians claim the first figure's four moods always yield a necessary conclusion. Critics object: if the conclusion is coextensive with a premise, it's not a genuine consequence; if not coextensive, no necessary connection. The author answers by distinguishing types of conjunction and proving coextensive consequences exist, using the natural example of sunrise and daylight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's dilemma
Extract the two horns: either the necessary consequence (conclusion) is coextensive with the antecedent (impossible for a consequence) or it is not coextensive (no necessary connection). Underline the logical move: the objector tries to force a contradiction.
The objector assumes a consequence must either be equal or unrelated.
2
Locate the author's response to the first horn
Find where the author denies the impossibility of coextensiveness. The author argues that a consequence can be coextensive, using the example of sunrise and daylight: they are inseparable yet one is the consequence of the other.
This refutes the claim that coextensiveness negates consequence.
3
Examine the response to the second horn
The objector claims lack of conjunction (iqtiran) proves absence of necessity. The author denies this: constant conjunction does not prevent consequence; rather, absolute conjunction (without regard to cause/effect) would prevent it.
The author distinguishes between types of conjunction.
4
Analyse the sunrise-daylight example in Islamic context
Notice the example from the natural world: sunrise (cause) and daylight (effect) are constantly conjoined. The author argues daylight is a necessary consequence of sunrise despite being coextensive. Relate this to kalam arguments about causality.
In kalam, similar reasoning defends necessary connections in proofs for God.
5
Assess the validity of the objection and answer
Consider: Does the author fully rebut the objection? The answer is incomplete in the text, but the first part is sound. The objector's dilemma is logically refuted by showing a counterexample. Evaluate whether the author's distinction between types of conjunction is adequate.
The dialectic continues with further objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول: إنَّ المنتج في هذا الشكل من الضُّروب الأربعة لا يكون منتجا لذاته فإن من الوجوه ما ينافيه نحو الوجوه المذكورة في الشكل الأول وغيرها. وذلك من وجوه أيضًا: (۱) منها أن يقال هذا اللازم لا يلزم عنه لذاته فإنَّه إذا لزم فلا يخلو من أن يكون مساويا له : وإنَّه باطل ضرورة كونه لازما، واللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره. أو لا يكون مساويًا، وإنَّه باطل أيضًا ضرورة عدم الاقتران بينهما أصلا وإلا لا يكون لازما أبدًا. (۲) ومنها أن يقال إنَّه لا يلزم عنه لذاته فإنه إذا لزم: فإما أن يلزم من الغير وإنَّه محال فإنه يفتقر وجوده إلى وجود أحد ملزوميه حينئذ؛ وذلك هو الغير. ولو كان مفتقرًا إلى الغير فلا يلزم عنه لذاته. وإما أن لا يلزم من الغير، و إنَّه محال أيضًا فإنه يلزم من الضرب الثاني في الشكل الأول: وذلك غيره. (۳) ومنها أن يقال إنَّه لا يلزم عنه لذاته؛ إذ الموضوع في النتيجة وهي قولنا لا شيء من «ج» «أ» لا يخلو من أن يكون غير المحمول أو لا يكون، وإنما قد لا يكون هذا القول مختصا بذلك الضَّرب ولا يكون مفتقرا صدقه في الذهن إلى صدق ذلك كذلك وهذا ظاهر. (٤) ومنها أنْ يقال إنَّه لا يلزم عنه لذاته إلَّا وأن يكون الأوسط فيه متّحدا، والأوسط فيه متّحدا وإلا لكان متحدا في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الجماد حيوان وليس كذلك فإن الحيوان في الصغرى والكبرى منكرا منكرا ، ومن اللوازم أنْ يكون المنكر الثَّاني في اللفظ غير الأول أما سمعت قول القائل أن بلغت عشر ... (٥) ومنها أن يقال إنَّه إذا لزم عنه لذاته كان من اللوازم أنْ يلزم في سائر الصُّور؛ وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كلُّ إنسان متنفّس ولا شيء من المستنشق بمتنفس؛ فلا يلزم منه لا شيء من الإنسان بمستنشق. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية. لكنا نقول في الجواب (۱) عن الأوَّل إنّه لا يمكن أن يكون مساويًا به وإن كان لازما للغير. فإن من الأشياء ما يكون لازما للشَّيء وغيره لازما مساويًا الملزومه، وأما قوله اللازم للغير يمكن أن يوجد عند وجود ذلك الغير دون وجود غيره فذلك في حيّز المنع لأنَّه لا يمكن إلا وأن يمكن الاقتران بينهما في التَّحقق. ومن الجائز أن لا يمكن كما في طلوع الشمس ووجود النَّهار فإنه لا يمكن أن ينفك أحدهما عن الآخر وقد كان ضوء العالم لهذا؛ ولذلك كان لازما مساويًا للملزوم. وأما قوله في القسم الآخر ضرورة عدم الاقتران بينهما وإلا لا يكون لازما فذلك في حيّز المنع أيضًا. فإن الاقتران بينهما من حيث هو الاقتران بينهما لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم، والاقتران بين اللازم والملزوم لا يكون مانعا عن اللزوم بل المانع هو الاقتران بينهما على الإطلاق من غير أن ينظر إلى أحدهما بأنَّه لازم أو ملزوم. ولا يقال كيف يمكن أن يكون الأعم مانعا عن الشَّيء، والأخصُّ منه لا يكون مانعا عن ذلك فإن الأعم وهو الإنسان مثلا مانع عما يكون مانعا عن كونه أعم، والأخصّ وهو هذا الإنسان لا يكون
Then one may say: The product in this figure from the four moods is not productive by itself, for there are aspects that contradict it, such as the aspects mentioned in the first figure and others. And from the aspects also: (1) that it is said: This necessary consequence does not follow from it by itself, for if it follows, it must either be coextensive with it — and that is false by necessity because it is a consequence, and the consequence of something else can exist when that something else exists without [needing] something else — or it is not coextensive, and that is also false by necessity because of the lack of conjunction between the two at all, otherwise it would never be a consequence. (2) ... (3) ... (4) ... (5) ... But we say in answer (1) to the first: it cannot be coextensive with it even if it is a consequence of something else. For some things are necessary consequences of a thing and other consequences are coextensive with their subject. As for his saying: the consequence of something else can exist when that something else exists without something else — that is subject to denial, because it cannot be unless the conjunction between them in actual existence is possible. And it is possible that it cannot, as in the rising of the sun and the existence of daylight, for one cannot be separated from the other, and the light of the world is due to this; therefore it is a consequence coextensive with its subject. And as for his saying in the other part, 'the necessity of lack of conjunction between them otherwise it would not be a consequence' — that is also subject to denial. For the conjunction between them in terms of being a conjunction does not prevent consequence, and the conjunction between the necessary consequence and its subject does not prevent consequence; rather the preventer is absolute conjunction without regard to which is consequence or subject. And it is not said how can the more general prevent something, while the more specific does not prevent such? For the more general, e.g. 'human', prevents what prevents it from being more general, while the specific, e.g. this human, does not...
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange (jadal) concerning the productivity of the four moods of the first figure of categorical syllogism. The objector raises five arguments (the full five are listed but only the first is answered on page 43). The first objection argues that the necessary consequence (the conclusion) does not follow necessarily because either the consequence is coextensive with the antecedent (which is impossible for a necessary consequence) or it is not coextensive (which would mean no necessary connection). The author's answer refutes both horns of the dilemma. For the first horn, the author denies the impossibility of coextensiveness; there are necessary consequences that are coextensive, such as daylight following sunrise — they are inseparable yet one is still the consequence of the other. For the second horn, the author denies that lack of conjunction (i.e., that they are sometimes separate) is required for consequence; rather, constant conjunction does not prevent consequence. The author uses the example of sunrise and daylight to illustrate coextensive consequence. The answer continues discussing the preventer of consequence, but it is cut off. This lesson covers the first objection and its incomplete answer.

Key Takeaway

Fourth Objection: Identity of the Middle Term and the Author's Reply
Objection and reply about middle term identity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the objector's claim about the middle term's identity and explain how the author defends the logical unity of the middle term despite linguistic variation.

Core Idea
Middle term identity depends on conceptual essence, not linguistic form.

In syllogistic logic, the middle term must be identical in both premises for the syllogism to be productive. An objector raises a challenge: in the example 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal', the word 'animal' appears indefinite and thus not truly identical. How can the syllogism still be valid? The author's response clarifies the distinction between linguistic form and logical essence. Let's dissect this dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
Locate the objector's statement: 'It does not imply by itself except that the middle term is unified.' The objector argues that in the given example, the middle term 'animal' is not unified because it appears indefinite (nakira) in both premises.
The objector assumes that the middle term must be identical in wording, not just in meaning.
2
Examine the objector's example
The objector cites the syllogism: 'Every human is an animal; no stone is an animal.' He claims that 'animal' in the minor and major is indefinite, and the second occurrence is lexically different from the first.
The objection hinges on the linguistic form of 'animal' as an indefinite noun.
3
Extract the author's response
The author responds: 'The middle term in the examples you gave is 'animal' insofar as it is animal.' He distinguishes between 'animal' considered in its essence (min haythu huwa hayawan) and 'animal' qualified as 'animal such-and-such' (with additional description).
The author argues that the essence of 'animal' is identical in both premises; any linguistic variation is incidental.
4
Understand the linguistic vs. logical distinction
The author explicitly states: 'As for the indefinite in the second instance being different from the first, that is according to language; but our discussion here is according to technical terminology, so it does not affect.'
This shows that identity in logic is based on the concept (ma'na), not the word (lafz).

(٤) ومنها أنْ يقال إنَّه لا يلزم عنه لذاته إلَّا وأن يكون الأوسط فيه متّحدا، والأوسط فيه متّحدا وإلا لكان متحدا في قولنا كل إنسان حيوان ولا شيء من الجماد حيوان وليس كذلك فإن الحيوان في الصغرى والكبرى منكرا منكرا ، ومن اللوازم أنْ يكون المنكر الثَّاني في اللفظ غير الأول أما سمعت قول القائل أن بلغت عشر ... وعن الرابع إنَّ الأوسط فيما ضربتم من الأمثال هو الحيوان من حيث إنه حيوان إلَّا أَنَّه حيوان كذا، والحيوان من حيث هو الحيوان لا يمكن أن يكون في الصغرى غير ما في الكبرى أو بالعكس. وأمَّا أنَّ المنكر في المرة الثانية غير ما في الأولى فذلك بحسب اللغة؛ والكلام فيما نحن فيه بحسب الاصطلاح فلا يقدح فيه.
(4) Among them is that it is said: It does not imply by itself except that the middle term in it is unified, but the middle term is unified; otherwise it would be unified in our statement 'Every human is an animal' and 'No stone is an animal', and it is not so, for 'animal' in the minor and major is indefinite indefinite, and among the necessary consequences is that the second indefinite in wording is not the first – have you not heard the speaker's saying 'that he reached ten...'? And regarding the fourth: The middle term in the examples you gave is 'animal' insofar as it is animal, except that it is animal such-and-such, and 'animal' insofar as it is animal cannot be in the minor different from that in the major, or vice versa. As for the indefinite in the second instance being different from the first, that is according to language; but our discussion here is according to technical terminology, so it does not affect.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth objection concerning the condition that the middle term be identical in both premises for a syllogism to produce a conclusion by itself. The objector claims that in the example 'Every human is an animal' and 'No stone is an animal', the middle term 'animal' appears indefinite in both premises (perhaps as an indefinite noun), yet it is not truly identical, so the syllogism should not be productive. He adds that the second indefinite is lexically different. The author responds by explaining that the middle term 'animal' is considered identical in essence (from the aspect of being animal), even if it is qualified differently (e.g., 'animal such-and-such'). The difference in wording (indefinite vs. indefinite) is merely linguistic, not logical; in logical terminology, the middle term is the same. Therefore, the objection does not refute the syllogism's validity. This clarifies that the identity of the middle term is based on its conceptual essence, not its linguistic form.

The middle term's identity is determined by its conceptual essence, not by its linguistic form, preserving the validity of categorical syllogisms despite grammatical variation.

The Fifth Objection: Must a Syllogism Work in All Forms?
Objection that if productive by itself, it should work in all forms; author's reply about customary usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a violation of the distribution rule in a categorical syllogism and explain why a counterexample does not disprove the intrinsic productivity of the first figure.

Core Idea
A syllogism's form must satisfy distribution rules; a flawed counterexample does not refute intrinsic productivity.

The fifth objection challenges the claim that the first figure syllogism is productive by its very nature. The objector presents a syllogism that, despite universal premises, fails to produce a conclusion. The author's reply invokes the distribution condition and the customary meaning of universal statements. This lesson dissects the logical flaw in the objection and reinforces the importance of proper form.

Guided Path
1
State the objector's claim
Find the phrase in the Arabic: 'if it implies by itself, it would be necessary that it imply in all forms'. This is the objector's main point: intrinsic productivity should hold across all syllogistic forms.
2
Examine the counterexample
The objector gives: 'Every human is breathing, no inhaler is breathing, therefore no human is an inhaler.' Write the premises and conclusion. Note that the terms are: human (minor), inhaler (major), breathing (middle).
The example is intended to show that the conclusion does not follow, but this is disputed.
3
Analyze middle term distribution
In 'Every human is breathing' (universal affirmative), the predicate 'breathing' is undistributed. In 'No inhaler is breathing' (universal negative), the predicate 'breathing' is distributed. The middle term is distributed in only one premise, which is actually sufficient, but the objection claims the syllogism fails. This suggests the premises may not be understood as standard categorical propositions.
The author's reply about customary usage ('āda) clarifies that 'Every J is B' means 'every J is B at the time it is B', implying a temporal condition that affects logical form.
4
Understand the author's reply
The author says the objection has already been addressed and points to the customary intention behind universal statements. The syllogism's failure is due to not respecting the intended meaning of the premises, not because the first figure lacks intrinsic productivity.
5
Conclude the logical lesson
The objection is invalid because the counterexample does not meet the conditions of a valid categorical syllogism. The first figure's productivity remains intact when premises are correctly formed and distributed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(٥) ومنها أن يقال إنَّه إذا لزم عنه لذاته كان من اللوازم أنْ يلزم في سائر الصُّور؛ وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كلُّ إنسان متنفّس ولا شيء من المستنشق بمتنفس؛ فلا يلزم منه لا شيء من الإنسان بمستنشق. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية. وعن الخامس إنَّه من جملة ما قد مرّ الكلام فيه ولأن العادة جرث في قولهم كلُّ «ج» «ب» إنَّه إنَّما يستعمل ذلك على قصد أنَّ كلَّ «ج» «ب» حين ما يكون «ب» فيجب أن يعتبر هذا في هذا الشكل.
(5) Among them is that it is said: If it implies by itself, then it would be necessary that it imply in all forms, and it is not so – for if we say 'Every human is breathing' and 'No inhaler is breathing', it does not imply 'No human is an inhaler'. And similarly in the remaining moods. And regarding the fifth: It is among that which has already been discussed, and because the custom goes in their statement 'Every J is B' that it is only used with the intention that every J is B when it is B, so this must be considered in this figure.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its answer. The objector argues that if a syllogism were productive by its very nature (li-dhatihi), it should yield a conclusion in all forms without exception. He gives a counterexample: 'Every human is breathing' and 'No inhaler is breathing' does not produce 'No human is an inhaler' – since 'inhaler' (mustanfiq) is not a proper term? Actually, the example seems flawed, but the objector uses it to claim that the syllogism is not productive. The author responds that this objection has already been addressed (likely referring to earlier discussions) and further explains that in the standard usage of 'Every J is B', it is intended that every J is B at the time it is B (i.e., with temporal condition). Therefore, the syllogism in the first figure must be considered with this condition. In the example, 'every human is breathing' is true, and 'no inhaler is breathing' may also be true, but the conclusion does not follow because 'inhaler' and 'human' may not be related as required. The point is that the syllogism's productivity requires adherence to the logical form where the middle term is properly distributed and the premises are taken as universal affirmative and negative in the standard way.

Key Takeaway
A syllogism's intrinsic productivity depends on proper logical form and the intended meaning of its propositions; a counterexample that violates these conditions does not refute the principle.

Why Difference in Quality is Necessary in Categorical Syllogisms
Question why syllogism productivity requires difference in quality between premises, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two categorical premises differ in quality and evaluate the validity of an objection that claims productivity without such difference.

Core Idea
A productive syllogism needs a negative premise for a negative conclusion; apparent exceptions conceal a quality difference.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a productive categorical syllogism requires a difference in quality between the premises under certain conditions. An interlocutor now raises examples that seem to produce a valid conclusion without any difference in quality. In this lesson, we will analyze these examples carefully and see that the condition is actually met, but the quality of one premise is misidentified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in the first example
The example: 'Every writer is moving' and 'Every stationary is not moving'. Write down both premises exactly as given.
Note the use of 'not moving' as a predicate.
2
Determine the quality (kayf) of each premise
In categorical logic, a premise is affirmative if the predicate is affirmed of the subject, and negative if the predicate is denied. 'Every writer is moving' is affirmative (A). 'Every stationary is not moving' denies movement of stationary things, so it is universal negative (E).
'Not moving' is a single predicate, but the copula 'is not' makes it negative.
3
Check if there is a difference in quality
One premise is A, the other is E, so they differ in quality. The interlocutor mistakenly thought both were affirmative because he considered 'not moving' as an affirmative predicate. In standard logic, the quality is determined by the copula.
If we incorrectly treat 'not moving' as an affirmative, we miss the difference.
4
Evaluate the second example similarly
The second example: 'Every jurist is necessarily knowledgeable' (affirmative modal) and 'Every fool is knowledgeable not necessarily'. The second premise is a negative modal: it says the fool is not necessarily knowledgeable, which is a denial of necessity. So again, the premises differ in quality (one affirmative necessity, one negative possibility).
Modal propositions also have quality based on affirmation/negation of the copula.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قيل سلَّمنا أنَّ ما ذكر تم منتج لذاته لكن لم قلتمْ إنَّ إنتاجه يفتقر إلى الاختلاف بين مقدمتين في الكيف؟ وكيف يفتقر وقد لزم في كثير من الصُّور بدونه كما إذا قلت كلُّ كاتب متحرك وكلُّ ساكن لا متحرك فلا شيء من الكاتب بساكن؟ وكذلك إذا قلت كل فقيه عالم بالضّرورة وكلُّ سفيه عالم لا بالضرورة فلا واحد من الفقيه بسفيه. وعلى هذا فإنه يتعدد حسب تعدُّد المعدولات وذوات الجهات فنقول مثل هذه الاعتراضات، وإنْ كان مسطورًا في بعض من الكتب فلا يكون كما
And if it is said: We grant that what you mentioned is productive by itself, but why do you say that its production requires difference between the two premises in quality? And how does it require it when it has occurred in many forms without it, as when you say 'Every writer is moving' and 'Every stationary is not moving' – then 'No writer is stationary'? And similarly if you say 'Every jurist is necessarily knowledgeable' and 'Every fool is knowledgeable not necessarily' – then 'No jurist is a fool'. And so it varies according to the variation of modified (ma'dula) propositions and those with modality. We say such objections, even if recorded in some books, are not as...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage presents a new query from an interlocutor who accepts the earlier claim about productivity by itself but questions the necessity of a difference in quality (affirmative vs. negative) between the two premises for a productive syllogism. The interlocutor provides counterexamples: (1) 'Every writer is moving' (affirmative) and 'Every stationary is not moving' (negative equivalent) – these are both universal, but one is affirmative and the other negative? Actually, 'Every stationary is not moving' is universal negative in meaning, so there is a difference in quality. Wait, the interlocutor claims these are productive without difference? The example 'Every writer is moving' (A) and 'Every stationary is not moving' (E) – these differ in quality: A is affirmative, E is negative. So the example actually has a difference in quality. Perhaps the interlocutor is counting 'Every stationary is not moving' as affirmative? That would be a mistake. The second example: 'Every jurist is necessarily knowledgeable' (modal affirmative) and 'Every fool is knowledgeable not necessarily' (modal negative? Actually it says 'not necessarily', which is a negation of modality). The interlocutor claims such syllogisms are productive even without a difference in quality? But these examples do have a difference: one is affirmative necessity, the other is negative possibility? The author seems to be responding that such objections are not valid. The text ends with 'we say such objections, even if recorded in some books, are not as...' The conclusion is cut off, but the implication is that the objections are flawed because they either misapply the condition or use examples that actually satisfy it.

Key Terms
كيف (kayf): quality of a proposition (affirmation or negation)
معدولة (maʿdūla): modified proposition; predicate is negated (e.g., 'X is not-moving')
ذوات الجهات (dhawāt al-jihāt): modal propositions with modalities like necessity, possibility

The condition of quality difference in syllogisms is often misunderstood; careful identification of propositional quality resolves apparent counterexamples like those raised by the interlocutor.
