Negative vs Privative Propositions in First Figure Syllogisms
Author discusses necessity of unified middle term and distinguishes negative from privative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a negative proposition (saliba) and a privative proposition (ma'dul) in the context of first-figure syllogisms, and explain why only the former yields a valid negative conclusion.

Core Idea
In the first figure, a privative cannot serve as a true negative.

In studying the first figure of categorical syllogisms, one crucial condition is that the middle term must be unified. However, a further nuance arises: the type of proposition used matters. This passage clarifies that a privative (ma'dul) such as 'lā mutaḥarrik' is not equivalent to a negative (sāliba) 'laysa bi-mutaḥarrik', and only the latter functions correctly in syllogistic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Locate the terms 'السالبة' and 'المعدول' in the Arabic. The negative is expressed as 'ليس بمتحرك', while the privative is 'لا متحرك'. Note the difference in structure: the privative uses a negated term (lā + noun) rather than negating the copula.
In Arabic grammar, 'laysa' is a verb of negation that negates the predicate, while 'lā' with a noun makes a privative term.
2
Understand the author's claim about equivalence
Read the sentence: 'ولا يلزم منه ما ذكرتم أيضًا وهو السالبة بل يلزم منه ذلك إذا كان قولنا لا متحرك بمعنى قولنا ليس بمتحرك'. The author says that the privative does not imply the negative unless it is interpreted as the negative. But that interpretation is invalid because the privative is 'غير محصل' (non-affirmative).
The author insists on a clear semantic boundary: the privative is not a genuine negation.
3
Analyze the consequence for the first figure
The author argues that if the privative were equivalent to the negative, it would fall under the first mood (الضرب الأول) of the first figure. But since it is not, the privative cannot serve as a proper proposition in a first-figure syllogism. Thus, the condition of a unified middle term fails if a privative is used as the negative premise.
This shows that proposition type is part of the condition for a valid syllogism.
4
Note the postponement of the second mood
The author defers discussion of the second mood to mixed syllogisms (المختلطات). This indicates that the classification of moods depends on proposition types and that the second mood may involve a different treatment of negatives.
The structure of the lesson suggests a systematic progression: first figure moods are understood sequentially.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينبغي إذ التركيب في الأوَّل لا يكون من التركيبات القياسية على الخصوص من التركيبات الواقعة في هذا الشكل. وقد كان من اللوازم أن يكون الأوسط متحدا في التركيبات القياسية وإنَّه لا يكون متحدا في ذلك ولا يلزم منه ما ذكرتم أيضًا وهو السالبة بل يلزم منه ذلك إذا كان قولنا لا متحرك بمعنى قولنا ليس بمتحرك. وليس كذلك لما مرَّ من قبل أنَّ المعدول غير المحصل ولا يمكنه أن يعنى به هذا فإنه إذا كان كذلك كان من جملة ما نحن في تحقيقه وهو الضّرب الأوّل منْ هذا الشكل. و أمَّا الثَّاني فذلك يعرف من بعد في المختلطات إنَّه هل هو من هذا الشَّكل أم لا ؟ والكلام في هذا المقام هو الكلام في الغير [؟].
It is necessary that the composition in the first [figure] is not from the qiyasi compositions especially among the compositions occurring in this figure. And it was among the necessities that the middle term be unified in the qiyasi compositions, and it is not unified in that, and what you mentioned does not follow from it, which is the negative, rather it follows from it when we say 'no moving' in the sense of 'it is not moving'. And it is not like that because of what has passed before, that the modified [proposition] is non-affirmative and cannot be meant by this, because if it were like that, it would be among what we are investigating, which is the first mood of this figure. As for the second, that will be known later in the mixed [syllogisms], whether it is from this figure or not. And the discussion in this place is the discussion about the other [?].
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion about the conditions of the first figure of the categorical syllogism. The author begins by asserting that a valid composition in the first figure must not simply be any syllogistic composition, but specifically one that conforms to the rules of this figure. He then addresses a necessity: the middle term must be unified (muṭṭaḥid) in all valid syllogistic compositions. However, he notes that in the case under discussion, the middle term is not unified, and the opponent's claim that a negative conclusion follows does not hold. The author distinguishes between a privative expression (معدول, ma'dūl) like 'la mutaḥarrik' (no moving) and a genuine negation 'laysa bi-mutaḥarrik' (it is not moving). Only the latter yields a true negative proposition. The privative is 'non-affirmative' (ghayr muḥaṣṣal) and cannot serve as a proper negative in the syllogism, because if it did, it would fall under the first mood of this figure. The author then postpones discussion of the second mood to the section on mixed syllogisms, where it will be determined whether it belongs to this figure. The passage concludes with an obscure remark about 'the other' (al-ghayr), possibly referring to another figure or mood. This passage requires careful parsing of the terms 'saliba' (negative), 'ma'dūl' (privative), and 'muḥaṣṣal' (affirmative), and it highlights the importance of precise proposition types in syllogistic logic.

Key Terms
الأوسط (al-awsaṭ): The middle term in a categorical syllogism, which must be unified (muttaḥid) across both premises for a valid inference. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable.]
السالبة (al-sāliba): A negative proposition (e.g., 'laysa bi-mutaḥarrik'), as opposed to a privative (ma'dūl). [Note: In later texts, al-sāliba may also refer to a negative categorical proposition in the syllogism.]
المعدول (al-ma'dūl): A privative proposition that affirms or denies a privative term (e.g., 'lā mutaḥarrik' meaning 'non-moving'), which the author considers non-affirmative (ghayr muḥaṣṣal). [Note: In some later logicians, al-ma'dūl may be treated as equivalent to al-sāliba, but here they are sharply distinguished.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the middle term is not unified.
B) Because it is non-affirmative and cannot stand for a true negative.
C) Because it belongs to the second mood, not the first.
D) Because it is a mixed syllogism.

Key Takeaway
In first-figure syllogisms, a privative proposition (ma'dūl) cannot substitute for a true negative (sāliba) because it is non-affirmative, and using it violates the condition of a unified middle term.

The Syllogism

Uniform Predication and Syllogistic Transitivity
Author explains simple transitive predication and its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a chain of universal predications (al-qawl ʿalā al-mawḍūʿ) yields a valid conclusion by checking if the major term is predicated of all of the middle term.

Core Idea
Transitivity holds only when the major term covers the entire middle term.

The author begins with a simple rule: if you predicate something of something else universally, and then predicate that something of a third thing universally, then the first predicate applies to the third. This is the basis of categorical syllogisms. But as we shall see, this rule has a hidden condition: the major term must be truly universal over the middle. This lesson unpacks that condition using examples from the logic of species and genera.

Guided Path
1
Identify the chain of predications
Look at the given sequence: A is predicated of B as al-qawl ʿalā al-mawḍūʿ, and B is predicated of C in the same way. Does the author claim that A is then predicated of C?
Example: animal (A) on human (B), human (B) on Zaid (C).
2
Check if the major term covers all of the middle
For the conclusion to be valid, the major term (A) must be predicated of every individual that the middle term (B) covers. If A is only true of a part of B, the conclusion fails.
Example: rational (A) on some animal (B) – not all animals are rational, so even though animal (B) covers every horse (C), rational does not apply to horse.
3
Apply the condition to Islamic science examples
Test with fiqh examples: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabidh is an intoxicant.' The major term 'forbidden' covers all intoxicants, so it applies to nabidh. But if the major term were 'requires ablution' which applies only to some liquids (e.g., camel milk is not intoxicant), transitivity fails.
Use only Islamic science examples.
4
Formulate the rule for uniform predication
When all predications are of the type 'alā', the syllogism is valid only if the major term is predicated of every instance of the middle term. This is the condition of universal major premise (Barbara mood).
This is the first figure condition (Dici de omni).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في التأليفات الواقعة بين القول على الموضوع والوجود في الموضوع] وأما التأليفات الواقعة بين القول على الموضوع والوجود في الموضوع، فنقول إذا حمل شيء على شيء حمل المقول على الموضوع ثم حمل على ذلك الشيء شيء آخر كذلك. فإن هذا حمل على الذي حمل عليه الأول كما إذا حمل الحيوان على الإنسان حمل المقول على الموضوع وحمل الإنسان على زيد وعمر و كذلك. فالحيوان يحمل على زيد وعمرو حمل المقول على الموضوع أيضًا؛ إذ زيد حيوان ويشترك معه في حده أي يحمل عليه حده، ولا يقال كيف هو والجنس يحمل على الحيوان والحيوان يحمل على الإنسان؛ والجنس لا يحمل على الإنسان؛ إذ الجنس لا يحمل على طبيعة الحيوان حمل {على}، وطبيعته ليست بجنس أصلا وإلا لكان كل حيوان جنسا؛ إذ الحيوان الذي يحمل عليه الجنسية هو طبيعة الحيوان باعتبار تجريدها في الذهن بحيث يصلح لإيقاع الشركة فيها. وهذا الاعتبار أخص من اعتبار الحيوان بما هو حيوان فقط لما أنه بشرط التجريد دون ذلك. وإنما يقال عليه الجنسية إذا اعتبر في الذهن بشرط الخلط بالقول وصول خلط بالقوة، ولم يكن الحيوان بهذا الشرط، وبشرط التجريد مقولا على الإنسان؛ بل بلا شرط خلط، والجنسية عرض في هذه الطبيعة موجودة فيها وجود الشيء في الموضوع. وأما الجنس فقوله على ما يقال عليه من هذه الطبيعة قول المركب من العرض، والحامل على الموضوع له مثل قول الأبيض على زيد. وبالحقيقة أن هذا يرجع إلى أن الطرف الأكبر يحمل على بعض الأوسط وعلى البعض الذي لا يحمل على الطرف الأصغر، وبحسب أن يعتبر المقول «على» والموجود «في كليا ، فإنه إذا كان جزئياً حتى يكون الطرف الأكبر على بعض الأوسط لم يجب في اتفاق القولين على أن يقال الطرف الأكبر على الأصغر. فإن الناطق يحمل على بعض الحيوان بـ«على» والحيوان يحمل على كل فرس ب على، ولا يلزم أن يحمل الناطق على الفرس بـ«على»؛ ولو كان يدل الجنس شيء هو في حكم الجنس من حيث العموم وكان يحمل على كل الأوسط ما يمتنع أن يحمل على ما تحت الأوسط، وإن النسب مختلفة كلها، فالطرف الأكبر مقول على الأوسط والأوسط موجود في الأصغر. والمشهور فيه أن يقال إنه لا يحمل على هذا ولا في ذلك كذلك. فإن اللون يحمل على الأبيض حمل على، والأبيض يحمل على القَقنَّسِ حمل في، واللون لا يحمل على القَقنَّسِ حمل على، ولا حمل في أيضًا. ومنهم من حاول فيما قاله أن يصلح، فقال إنه في بعض المواضع هكذا وفي البعض الآخر يحمل كما إذا قلت الأبيض على أبيض ما لما أنه ذاتي وأبيض ما موجود في معان ما. ثم نقول للبيضائي إنه أبيض والأبيض موجود في البيضائي إذا اعتقدت أنه وصفٌ عرضي، والحق فيه أن الأول يكون موجودًا في الثالث على كل حال، فإن الشيء إذا كان فيه اللون الأبيض كان فيه جميع الأمور التي يقال على اللون قولا كليا، وإلا لكان فيه بياض ولم يكن فيه لون، ولكن إذا كان ذلك المعنى ما يقال على العرض وعلى موضوعه فلا يمتنع أن يقال على موضوعه لا من جهة العرض؛ بل الذي من جهة العرض لا يقال عليه كما إذا كان الواحد مثلا يقال على العرض / .
Section: On the compositions that occur between predication of the subject and existence in the subject. As for the compositions that occur between predication of the subject and existence in the subject, we say: when something is predicated of something with the predication of the subject (al-qawl 'alā al-mawḍū'), and then something else is predicated of that thing similarly, then this is a predication of that which was predicated first. For example, when animal is predicated of human with the predication of the subject, and human is predicated of Zaid and Amr similarly, then animal is predicated of Zaid and Amr also with the predication of the subject, because Zaid is an animal and shares with it in its definition, i.e., its definition applies to him. And do not say: how is that? The genus is predicated of animal, and animal is predicated of human, but the genus is not predicated of human. Indeed, the genus is not predicated of the nature of animal with predication of the subject, and its nature is not genus at all, otherwise every animal would be a genus. Indeed, animal that bears genus-ness is the nature of animal considered abstractly in the mind such that it is fit for participation in it. This consideration is more specific than considering animal merely as animal, because it is conditioned by abstraction without that. It is called genus-ness only if it is considered in the mind with the condition of mixing with the [?] and the arrival of mixing in potentiality, and animal is not with this condition. And with the condition of abstraction it is predicated of human, but without the condition of mixing. And genus-ness is an accident in this nature existing in it with the existence of a thing in a subject. As for genus, it is a predication of what is predicated of this nature, a composite expression of accident, and predicated of its subject, like the predication of white on Zaid. In reality, this reduces to the fact that the major term is predicated of some of the middle and of some that is not predicated of the minor term. And depending on whether the predication 'alā and existence fī are considered universal, if it is particular so that the major is of some of the middle, it is not necessary, even if the two predications agree, that the major be predicated of the minor. For rational is predicated of some animal via 'alā, and animal is predicated of every horse via 'alā, but it does not follow that rational is predicated of horse via 'alā. And if the genus indicated something that is in the category of genus in terms of generality and was predicated of all of the middle, it would be impossible for it to be predicated of what is under the middle. And the relations are all different: the major term is predicated of the middle via 'alā, and the middle exists in the minor via fī. The well-known case is that it is said: it is not predicated via 'alā nor via fī in that way. For color is predicated of white via 'alā, and white exists in qaqannas via fī, and color is not predicated of qaqannas via 'alā, nor via fī either. And some of them tried to correct what he said, saying that in some places it is like this and in others it is predicated, as when you say white about something white because it is essential, and something white exists in certain things. Then we say about the white thing that it is white and white exists in the white thing if you believe it is an accidental description. The truth is that the first (major) exists in the third (minor) in any case, because if a thing has the color white, it has all the things that are predicated of color universally. Otherwise, it would have whiteness but not color. But if that meaning is predicated of the accident and of its subject, it is not impossible for it to be predicated of its subject not from the aspect of the accident. Rather, that from the aspect of the accident is not predicated of it, as when the one, for example, is predicated of the accident.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical compositions that arise when combining the two types of predication: predication of the subject (al-qawl 'alā al-mawḍū') and existence in the subject (al-wujūd fī al-mawḍū'). The author aims to show how the validity of syllogisms depends on the uniformity of predication type. He begins by stating a general rule: if something is predicated of something else as a universal (qawl 'alā al-mawḍū'), and then that something is predicated of a third thing in the same way, the first predication applies to the third. This is a straightforward transitive case, illustrated with the example: animal is predicated of human (universal), human is predicated of Zaid (individual), so animal is predicated of Zaid. This works because all predications are of the same type ('alā). Next, the author addresses a potential confusion about genus (jins). One might think that since genus (e.g., animal) is predicated of its species (human), and the genus of the genus (e.g., body? but here it's unclear) would not be predicated of the species. He clarifies that genus-ness is an accident that attaches to the nature of animal when considered abstractly in the mind with the condition of being free from mixing. The nature itself, without this condition, is not a genus. Therefore, the genus as a composite of accident and substance (like 'white' predicated of Zaid) is different from the simple predication. The discussion then shifts to the core issue: when the major term (al-ṭarf al-akbar) is predicated of only some of the middle term (al-awsaṭ) via 'alā, and the middle is predicated of all the minor (al-aṣghar) via 'alā, the major may not apply to the minor. The example: 'rational' (nāṭiq) is predicated of some animal (humans) via 'alā, and 'animal' is predicated of every horse via 'alā, but 'rational' is not predicated of horse. This shows that transitivity fails when the major is not a universal predicate over the entire middle. The author then considers a mixed case: the major is predicated of the middle via 'alā, but the middle exists in the minor via fī (as an accident). Here, the major is not predicated of the minor via either mode. Example: 'color' is predicated of 'white' via 'alā, but 'white' exists in a substance (qaqannas) via fī. Then 'color' is not predicated of that substance via 'alā nor via fī. Some philosophers attempted to fix this by suggesting that in certain cases, when the middle is essential (dhātī) to the minor, the major may still apply. But the author rejects this and states the truth: the major always exists in the minor in some sense because if a thing has a specific accident (e.g., whiteness), it necessarily has the higher genus (color). However, the predication 'alā from the aspect of the accident does not hold for the subject directly; it only holds via the accident. This clarifies the conditions under which mixed predications yield valid or invalid syllogisms.

Key Terms
القول على الموضوع
المقولة على الموضوع
الطرف الأكبر
الطرف الأوسط
الطرف الأصغر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the lesson's example, why does 'rational' not apply to 'every horse' even though 'animal' applies to every horse?
A) Because 'rational' is not a universal term.
B) Because 'rational' is predicated of only some animals (humans), not all of the middle term 'animal'.
C) Because 'horse' is not a rational animal.
D) Because the predication of 'animal' on 'horse' uses existence in the subject, not predication of the subject.

Key Takeaway
A categorical syllogism with uniform predication (alā) is valid only when the major term is truly universal over the entire middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid categorical syllogism in the first figure using an example from fiqh: all intoxicants are forbidden, all nabidh is an intoxicant, therefore nabidh is forbidden. Now modify the major premise to a particular: some intoxicants are forbidden (e.g., only those that intoxicate heavily). Does the conclusion still follow? Why not? Explain using the condition taught in this lesson.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Mixed Predication and Syllogistic Invalidity
Author analyzes mixed predication and its effect on syllogistic validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a syllogism's premises combine predication of the subject (alā) and existence in the subject (fī), and determine whether the conclusion is valid or not.

Core Idea
When predication types mix, the major term does not transfer to the minor through either mode.

In the previous lesson we saw that even uniform predication can fail if the major is not universal. But what happens when the two premises use different types of predication? The author now examines a mixed case: the major is predicated of the middle via alā, but the middle exists in the minor via fī. He shows that the major does not become predicated of the minor in either way. He then refutes an attempted correction and explains the underlying truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of predication in the premises
One premise uses predication of the subject (alā), the other uses existence in the subject (fī). In the example: 'color is predicated of white via alā' and 'white exists in qaqannas via fī'.
2
Check if the conclusion follows in either mode
The author says that in such mixed cases, the major is not predicated of the minor via alā nor via fī. Color is not predicated of qaqannas by alā (since qaqannas is not a color) nor by fī (since color is not an accident in qaqannas directly; only whiteness is).
3
Examine the attempted correction and refutation
Some philosophers tried to fix this by saying that the predication works if the middle is essential to the minor. The author rejects this, but then gives the truth: the major actually exists in the minor in a sense, because if the minor has a specific accident (whiteness), it necessarily has the higher genus (color). However, this does not give predication via alā or fī in the strict sense.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic science example
Consider a usul al-fiqh example: 'Riba (usury) is forbidden in wheat (alā predication). Wheat exists in bread via fī (as an ingredient). Does that make riba forbidden in bread? Not directly via the same predication. The ruling transfers only if the 'illah (cause) is present in the bread in the same way.

Commentary
This passage discusses the logical compositions that arise when combining the two types of predication: predication of the subject (al-qawl 'alā al-mawḍū') and existence in the subject (al-wujūd fī al-mawḍū'). The author aims to show how the validity of syllogisms depends on the uniformity of predication type. He begins by stating a general rule: if something is predicated of something else as a universal (qawl 'alā al-mawḍū'), and then that something is predicated of a third thing in the same way, the first predication applies to the third. This is a straightforward transitive case, illustrated with the example: animal is predicated of human (universal), human is predicated of Zaid (individual), so animal is predicated of Zaid. This works because all predications are of the same type ('alā). Next, the author addresses a potential confusion about genus (jins). One might think that since genus (e.g., animal) is predicated of its species (human), and the genus of the genus (e.g., body? but here it's unclear) would not be predicated of the species. He clarifies that genus-ness is an accident that attaches to the nature of animal when considered abstractly in the mind with the condition of being free from mixing. The nature itself, without this condition, is not a genus. Therefore, the genus as a composite of accident and substance (like 'white' predicated of Zaid) is different from the simple predication. The discussion then shifts to the core issue: when the major term (al-ṭarf al-akbar) is predicated of only some of the middle term (al-awsaṭ) via 'alā, and the middle is predicated of all the minor (al-aṣghar) via 'alā, the major may not apply to the minor. The example: 'rational' (nāṭiq) is predicated of some animal (humans) via 'alā, and 'animal' is predicated of every horse via 'alā, but 'rational' is not predicated of horse. This shows that transitivity fails when the major is not a universal predicate over the entire middle. The author then considers a mixed case: the major is predicated of the middle via 'alā, but the middle exists in the minor via fī (as an accident). Here, the major is not predicated of the minor via either mode. Example: 'color' is predicated of 'white' via 'alā, but 'white' exists in a substance (qaqannas) via fī. Then 'color' is not predicated of that substance via 'alā nor via fī. Some philosophers attempted to fix this by suggesting that in certain cases, when the middle is essential (dhātī) to the minor, the major may still apply. But the author rejects this and states the truth: the major always exists in the minor in some sense because if a thing has a specific accident (e.g., whiteness), it necessarily has the higher genus (color). However, the predication 'alā from the aspect of the accident does not hold for the subject directly; it only holds via the accident. This clarifies the conditions under which mixed predications yield valid or invalid syllogisms.

Checkpoint
In the example given in the passage, why is 'color' not predicated of 'qaqannas' via alā or via fī?
A) Because 'color' is not a genus of 'qaqannas'.
B) Because 'color' is predicated of 'white' via alā, but 'white' exists in 'qaqannas' via fī, and these two modes cannot combine to produce a predication of 'color' on 'qaqannas'.
C) Because 'qaqannas' is not white.
D) Because 'color' is an accident, not a substance.

Key Takeaway
Mixing predication of the subject (alā) and existence in the subject (fī) in a syllogism invalidates the conclusion, even if the major term is universal over the middle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following: in fiqh, 'wine is forbidden' (alā). 'Wine exists in vinegar via transformation' (fī, if it changes). Is the ruling of forbidden transferred to the vinegar? Use the lesson's rule to determine whether the conclusion holds or not, and explain your reasoning.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
The Condition of Universal Predication of the Major Term
First portion: rational/animal/horse example and the condition for universal predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a major term is not universally predicated of the middle term and assess whether a categorical syllogism's conclusion follows.

Core Idea
The major term must be predicated of every instance of the middle term for a valid conclusion.

In this passage, the author demonstrates that even if the middle term covers the minor term, the conclusion fails if the major term does not cover the entire middle. We will analyze the classic example of 'rational', 'animal', and 'horse' to understand this essential condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three terms
Find the major term (الطرف الأكبر), middle term (الأوسط), and minor term (الأصغر) in the example. The major is what is predicated in the conclusion, the middle connects, the minor is the subject.
Here: major = 'rational', middle = 'animal', minor = 'horse'.
2
Check universality of major over middle
Determine if the major term is predicated of every instance of the middle term. In the example, 'rational' is predicated of only some animals (e.g., humans), not all.
If the major is not universal over the middle, the syllogism is invalid even if the middle covers the minor.
3
Evaluate validity
Because 'rational' is not universal over 'animal', the conclusion 'rational is on every horse' does not follow. This is the author's point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا يرجع إلى أن الطرف الأكبر يحمل على بعض الأوسط وعلى البعض الذي لا يحمل على الطرف الأصغر، وبحسب أن يعتبر المقول «على» والموجود «في» كليا، فإنه إذا كان جزئياً حتى يكون الطرف الأكبر على بعض الأوسط لم يجب في اتفاق القولين على أن يقال الطرف الأكبر على الأصغر. فإن الناطق يحمل على بعض الحيوان بـ«على» والحيوان يحمل على كل فرس بـ«على»، ولا يلزم أن يحمل الناطق على الفرس بـ«على»؛ ولو كان يدل الجنس شيء هو في حكم الجنس من حيث العموم وكان يحمل على كل الأوسط ما يمتنع أن يحمل على ما تحت الأوسط، وإن النسب مختلفة كلها، فالطرف الأكبر مقول على الأوسط والأوسط موجود في الأصغر. والمشهور فيه أن يقال إنه لا يحمل على هذا ولا في ذلك كذلك. فإن اللون يحمل على الأبيض حمل على، والأبيض يحمل على القَقنَّسِ حمل في، واللون لا يحمل على القَقنَّسِ حمل على، ولا حمل في أيضًا.
ومنهم من حاول فيما قاله أن يصلح، فقال إنه في بعض المواضع هكذا وفي البعض الآخر يحمل كما إذا قلت الأبيض على أبيض ما لما أنه ذاتي وأبيض ما موجود في معان ما. ثم نقول للبيضائي إنه أبيض والأبيض موجود في البيضائي إذا اعتقدت أنه وصفٌ عرضي، والحق فيه أن الأول يكون موجودًا في الثالث على كل حال، فإن الشيء إذا كان فيه اللون الأبيض كان فيه جميع الأمور التي يقال على اللون قولا كليا، وإلا لكان فيه بياض ولم يكن فيه لون، ولكن إذا كان ذلك المعنى ما يقال على العرض وعلى موضوعه فلا يمتنع أن يقال على موضوعه لا من جهة العرض؛ بل الذي من جهة العرض لا يقال عليه كما إذا كان الواحد مثلا يقال على العرض / .
فيقال إن البياض واحد وكان الواحد مما يقال على البياض وعلى موضوعه، فإن الواحد حينئذ لا يمتنع أن يقال على الموضوع قول «على»، وليس من جملة البياض؛ لأن الواحد الذي قيل على البياض هو البياض. فإذن البياض في موضوعه فذلك الواحد ليس مقولا عليه حتى يكون من حيث ذلك البياض واحدًا؛ بل هو من جهة ذو واحد. وإن كان في نفسه واحدا فهو واحد أخرى فالواحد يقال على الموضوع في نفسه ويوجد فيه من جهة بياضه؛ إذ ذاك الواحد الذي هو البياض ليس هو الواحد الذي هو الموضوع؛ بل فيه، وهذا كالجوهر يقال على الإنسان ويقال على نفسه، والجوهر الذي هو نفسه لا يقال عليه؛ بل موجود فيه. وإن كان كوجود الجزء لا كوجود العرض فبين أنه لا يمتنع حينئذ أن الصفة مقولة عليه من جهة، ومقولة فيه من جهة. وأما إذا كان الوصف المقول على العرض خاصا به فإنه يكون موجودًا في الموضوع لا غير. وأما إذا قلت النسبة يجعل الطرف الأكبر موجودًا في الأوسط، فالمشهور فيه أنه تارة يحمل حمل «في» كالبياض في القَقنَّسِ، والققنَّس على فَقنَّس ما، والبياض في فَقنَّس ما؛ وتارة لا يحمل كالجنس في الحيوان والحيوان على الإنسان، والجنس لا يحمل على الإنسان ويجب أن ننظر فيما ذكرناه أن الجنس لا يحمل على الحيوان الذي هو بعينه مقول على الإنسان فلا يكون الأوسط واحدا بعينه، وإنما يجب أن نحفظ وحده الأوسط فإنه إذا كان واحدًا كان الموجود فيه إذا كان وجوده كليا كان هو موجودًا في الطرف الأصغر على خلاف أن لا يكون فيه كليًّا؛ بل يكون في بعضه، فإنه لا يجب ذلك أصلا. ثم الأكبر إذا كان على الأوسط، والأوسط على الأصغر غير ذاتي، فالأكبر أيضًا على الأصغر كالضحاك على كل إنسان، والماشي على كل ضحاك مختار، فالماشي على كل إنسان. وإن كان الطرف الأكبر موجودًا في الأوسط،
This goes back to the fact that the major term is predicated of some of the middle and of that which is not predicated of the minor. And according to considering the predicate 'on' and the existent 'in' as universal, for if it is particular so that the major term is on some of the middle, it is not necessary, even if the two statements agree, that the major term be said of the minor. For 'rational' is predicated of some animal by 'on', and animal is predicated of every horse by 'on', and it does not follow that 'rational' is predicated of horse by 'on'. And if the genus were to indicate something that is in the status of genus in terms of generality, and it were predicated of all the middle that which is prevented from being predicated of what is under the middle, and the relations are all different, then the major term is predicated of the middle and the middle exists in the minor. The common view is that it is said that it is not predicated of this nor in that either. For color is predicated of white by 'on' predication, and white is predicated of [swan] by 'in' predication, and color is not predicated of [swan] by 'on' predication, nor by 'in' predication either.
And some of them tried to fix what he said, so he said that in some places it is thus and in others it is predicated as when you say 'white' of something white because it is essential, and 'something white' exists in some meanings. Then we say of the white thing that it is white, and white exists in the white thing if you believe that it is an accidental description. The truth is that the first is existent in the third in every case; for if a thing has white color, it has all the attributes that are said of color universally; otherwise it would have whiteness without having color. But if that meaning is said of the accident and of its subject, then it is not prevented from being said of the subject not from the aspect of the accident; rather, that which is from the aspect of the accident is not said of it, as when 'one' for example is said of the accident.
Thus it is said that whiteness is one, and 'one' is among those things said of whiteness and of its subject, so 'one' then is not prevented from being said of the subject by 'on' predication, and it is not part of whiteness; because the 'one' that is said of whiteness is whiteness. Therefore whiteness is in its subject, so that 'one' is not predicated of it such that it is one by virtue of that whiteness; rather, it is from an aspect having one. And if it is one in itself, it is another one, so 'one' is said of the subject in itself and exists in it from the aspect of its whiteness; for that 'one' which is whiteness is not the 'one' which is the subject; rather, it is in it. This is like substance: it is said of man and said of itself, but the substance that is itself is not said of it; rather, it exists in it. And if it is like the existence of a part, not like the existence of an accident, then it is clear that then it is not prevented that the attribute be predicated of it from one aspect and predicated in it from another aspect. But when the description said of the accident is specific to it, then it exists in the subject only. And when you say the relation makes the major term existent in the middle, the common view is that sometimes it is predicated by 'in' predication, like whiteness in [swan], and [swan] on something swan-like, and whiteness in something swan-like; and sometimes it is not predicated, like genus in animal and animal on man, and genus is not predicated on man. And we must consider in what we mentioned that genus is not predicated of the animal that is itself predicated of man, so the middle is not one and the same. Rather, we must preserve the unity of the middle; for if it is one, then that which exists in it, if its existence is universal, then it is existent in the minor, contrary to if it is not universal in it; rather, it is in some of it, then that is not necessary at all. Then if the major is 'on' the middle, and the middle is 'on' the minor non-essentially, then the major is also 'on' the minor, like 'laughing' on every man, and 'walking' on every laughing being that chooses to walk, so 'walking' is on every man. And if the major term exists in the middle,
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the conditions under which the major term can be truly predicated of the minor term in a categorical syllogism, focusing on the distinction between essential predication (حمل 'على') and accidental inherence (حمل 'في'). The author begins by stating the problem: if the major term is predicated of only part of the middle term, and the middle term is predicated of some things that are not under the minor, then even if the premises agree, the conclusion does not necessarily follow. He illustrates with the example of 'rational' (الناطق) being predicated of some animals (حيوان) by 'on', and 'animal' being predicated of every horse (فرس) by 'on', yet 'rational' is not predicated of horse by 'on'. This shows that the major term must be universally predicated of the middle. Then he introduces another example: color (اللون) is predicated of white (الأبيض) by 'on', and white is predicated of a swan (القَقنَّس?) by 'in' (accidental inherence), but color is not predicated of the swan either by 'on' or by 'in'. This indicates that even if the middle term inheres in the minor accidentally, the major term may not inhere. The author then discusses an attempted fix: some say that in some cases the predication works, e.g., 'white' is essential to something white, and 'something white' exists in some meanings. He then refines this by distinguishing between what is said of the accident and what is said of its subject. For instance, 'one' (الواحد) is said of whiteness (البياض) and also of its subject, but the 'one' that is whiteness is not the same as the 'one' that is the subject; rather, the subject is one by virtue of having whiteness. He compares this to substance (الجوهر) being said of man and of itself, but the substance that is itself is not said of man; rather, it exists in man. The key point is that the attribute may be predicated of the subject from one aspect but exist in it from another. Finally, the author returns to the syllogistic condition: if the major term is 'on' the middle (essential predication), and the middle is 'on' the minor non-essentially, then the major is also 'on' the minor, as in the example: 'laughing' (الضحاك) is on every man, and 'walking' (الماشي) is on every laughing being that chooses to walk, so 'walking' is on every man. This demonstrates that the major term must be universally and essentially predicated of the middle term for a valid syllogism, whether the middle is essentially or accidentally predicated of the minor, as long as the predication is universal.

Key Terms
الطرف الأكبر
الأوسط
الأصغر
حمل على
حمل في

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the passage, why does 'rational' not apply to 'horse'?
A) Because 'rational' is not a species of 'horse'.
B) Because 'rational' is only predicated of some animals, not all.
C) Because 'horse' is not a rational animal.
D) Because the syllogism has four terms.

Key Takeaway
For a categorical syllogism to be valid, the major term must be predicated of every instance of the middle term.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Nabidh is an intoxicant. Therefore nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the major, minor, and middle terms. Check if the major term (forbidden) is universally predicated of the middle (intoxicant). Is this syllogism valid? Why?

The Syllogism
advanced

Essential vs Accidental Predication: The 'One' Example
Middle portion: attempted fix and the 'one' example illustrating mixed predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential predication (haml 'ala) from accidental inherence (haml fi) and determine when an attribute can be truly said of a subject even if it inheres accidentally.

Core Idea
An attribute may be predicated both essentially and accidentally, but the predication chain requires careful analysis of the aspect.

The author deepens the analysis by examining cases where an attribute like 'one' can be predicated of both an accident (whiteness) and its subject. He shows that the predication works if the attribute is not specific to the accident but applies to the subject in itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two predication types
Recall that 'haml 'ala' is essential predication (e.g., 'animal' of 'man') and 'haml fi' is accidental inherence (e.g., 'whiteness' in 'white body'). In the example, 'white' is predicated of swan by 'in', but 'color' is not predicated of swan at all.
2
Analyze the 'one' example
'One' is said of whiteness (by 'on') and also of the white subject (by 'on'? Actually, it can be said by 'on' as in 'the subject is one'). The author notes that the 'one' of whiteness is not the same as the 'one' of the subject; the subject is one by having whiteness.
This shows that an attribute can be predicated of the subject from a different aspect.
3
General condition
If the attribute is specific to the accident, it only exists in the subject (by 'in'), not predicated by 'on'. If it is general, it can be predicated by 'on'.

This indicates that even if the middle term inheres in the minor accidentally, the major term may not inhere. The author then discusses an attempted fix: some say that in some cases the predication works, e.g., 'white' is essential to something white, and 'something white' exists in some meanings. He then refines this by distinguishing between what is said of the accident and what is said of its subject. For instance, 'one' (الواحد) is said of whiteness (البياض) and also of its subject, but the 'one' that is whiteness is not the same as the 'one' that is the subject; rather, the subject is one by virtue of having whiteness. He compares this to substance (الجوهر) being said of man and of itself, but the substance that is itself is not said of man; rather, it exists in man. The key point is that the attribute may be predicated of the subject from one aspect but exist in it from another. Finally, the author returns to the syllogistic condition: if the major term is 'on' the middle (essential predication), and the middle is 'on' the minor non-essentially, then the major is also 'on' the minor, as in the example: 'laughing' (الضحاك) is on every man, and 'walking' (الماشي) is on every laughing being that chooses to walk, so 'walking' is on every man. This demonstrates that the major term must be universally and essentially predicated of the middle term for a valid syllogism, whether the middle is essentially or accidentally predicated of the minor, as long as the predication is universal.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Valid Syllogism with Non-essential Middle Predication
Final portion: condition for major term's predication and the laughing/walking example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that if the major term is essentially predicated of the middle, and the middle is universally predicated of the minor (even non-essentially), the conclusion follows.

Core Idea
A valid syllogism requires the major term to be universal over the middle, but the middle's predication over the minor can be accidental.

The author concludes with the example of 'laughing' and 'walking' to demonstrate that when the major term is 'on' the middle and the middle is 'on' the minor non-essentially, the major is still 'on' the minor. We will analyze this syllogism and the requirement of unity of the middle term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the syllogism's terms
Find the major term (walking), middle term (laughing), and minor term (man) in the example. The conclusion is 'walking is on every man'.
2
Check the premises
Major premise: Walking is on every laughing being. Minor premise: Laughing is on every man. Note that 'laughing' is predicated of man non-essentially (غير ذاتي).
The author says middle is 'on' the minor non-essentially, yet the conclusion follows.
3
Determine validity
Since the major term is universal over the middle, and the middle is universal over the minor (even accidentally), the conclusion is valid. This is a first-figure syllogism with universal affirmative premises.
The unity of the middle term must be preserved; if the middle is not one and the same, the syllogism fails.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical analysis of the conditions under which the major term can be truly predicated of the minor term in a categorical syllogism, focusing on the distinction between essential predication (حمل 'على') and accidental inherence (حمل 'في'). The author begins by stating the problem: if the major term is predicated of only part of the middle term, and the middle term is predicated of some things that are not under the minor, then even if the premises agree, the conclusion does not necessarily follow. He illustrates with the example of 'rational' (الناطق) being predicated of some animals (حيوان) by 'on', and 'animal' being predicated of every horse (فرس) by 'on', yet 'rational' is not predicated of horse by 'on'. This shows that the major term must be universally predicated of the middle. Then he introduces another example: color (اللون) is predicated of white (الأبيض) by 'on', and white is predicated of a swan (القَقنَّس?) by 'in' (accidental inherence), but color is not predicated of the swan either by 'on' or by 'in'. This indicates that even if the middle term inheres in the minor accidentally, the major term may not inhere. The author then discusses an attempted fix: some say that in some cases the predication works, e.g., 'white' is essential to something white, and 'something white' exists in some meanings. He then refines this by distinguishing between what is said of the accident and what is said of its subject. For instance, 'one' (الواحد) is said of whiteness (البياض) and also of its subject, but the 'one' that is whiteness is not the same as the 'one' that is the subject; rather, the subject is one by virtue of having whiteness. He compares this to substance (الجوهر) being said of man and of itself, but the substance that is itself is not said of man; rather, it exists in man. The key point is that the attribute may be predicated of the subject from one aspect but exist in it from another. Finally, the author returns to the syllogistic condition: if the major term is 'on' the middle (essential predication), and the middle is 'on' the minor non-essentially, then the major is also 'on' the minor, as in the example: 'laughing' (الضحاك) is on every man, and 'walking' (الماشي) is on every laughing being that chooses to walk, so 'walking' is on every man. This demonstrates that the major term must be universally and essentially predicated of the middle term for a valid syllogism, whether the middle is essentially or accidentally predicated of the minor, as long as the predication is universal.

The Syllogism
intermediate

Refuting the Impossibility of Accident in Accident
Critical refutation of a logical dogma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze and refute the claim that an accident cannot inhere in another accident using definitional critique and empirical counterexamples from Islamic natural philosophy.

Core Idea
Accidents can and do inhere in other accidents; the contrary claim is undefined and undemonstrated.

This passage tackles a common logical assertion that an accident cannot be predicated of another accident. The author dismantles this view by exposing its lack of definitional and demonstrative support, and provides concrete examples from the natural world. By studying this refutation, you will learn how to critically evaluate such universal negative claims using both logical analysis and real-world evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Claim Under Attack
Find the claim that the author refutes: that an accident cannot be in another accident (i.e., 'accident in accident' is impossible). Note that this is presented as a well-known view.
2
Examine the Syllogistic Framework
The author sets up a syllogism with major, middle, and minor terms to illustrate how predicates relate. Observe how the first scenario (non-essential predication) is accepted, while the second scenario (accident in accident) is disputed.
The example: 'Laughter of every man, and walker of every laughter, so walker of every man' is a valid non-essential predication.
3
Understand the Two-Pronged Critique
The author argues that the claim lacks both a proper definition (the definition of accident does not specify whether the subject is substance or accident) and a demonstration (no proof has been provided, nor can one be given).
4
Analyze the Counterexamples
The author provides examples of accidents that inhere in other accidents: contact (malābasa) inheres first in a surface (an accident of the body) and then in the body; time inheres in motion (an accident) before the moving body; color is predicated of blackness and whiteness, which are accidents.
These examples show that accident-in-accident is not only possible but common.
5
Conclude the Refutation
The examples demonstrate that the original claim is false. The author emphasizes that the subject of inherence can be a substance or another accident, and one does not exclude the other.
The passage concludes by distinguishing between subjects (substance) and loci (matter/form), but this is secondary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأكبر إذا كان على الأوسط، والأوسط على الأصغر غير ذاتي، فالأكبر أيضًا على الأصغر كالضحاك على كل إنسان، والماشي على كل ضحاك مختار، فالماشي على كل إنسان. وإن كان الطرف الأكبر موجودًا في الأوسط، والأوسط موجود في الأصغر فالمشهور فيه أنه ممتنع؛ وذلك لأن العرض لا يحمل على العرض. فإذا كان الأوسط عرضًا فالأكبر لا يمكن أن يكون عرضًا في الأوسط فيكون عرضًا في عرض. وهذا من جملة ما لم يجب من حد ولم يقم عليه برهان: إما أنه لا يجب من حد العرض فإن العرض يقال إنه الموجود في شيء بهذه الصفة ولم يبين أن ذلك الشيء هو جوهرًا وعرض. وإما أنه لا برهان عليه فلم يحاولوا هؤلاء إقامته في علومهم؛ ولا هو أيضًا في نفسه مما يقوم عليه البرهان فإن الحق هو ما يناقضه. وذلك لأن كثيرًا من الأعراض إنما يوجد في الجواهر بتوسط غيرها من الأعراض نحو الملابسة فإنها توجد في الجسم بتوسط / السطح، ولا يلزم من كونه موجودا [٤٠] في الجسم بتوسط أن يكون موجودًا في السطح أن لا يكون موجودًا في السطح؛ بل يمكن أن يجتمع القولان معًا لكن أحدهما أول، والثاني ثاني، فإن الملابسة يوجد في السطح أولا ثم في الجسم. وكذلك الزمان يعرض للحركة أولا ثم للمتحرك، وما يعرض في العرض يكون هو والعرض كلاهما في موضوع العرض كون الشيء في شيء منه فيكون بالحقيقة الموضوع لعلى والموضوع «في» لا يوجب أحدهما أن يكون هو الموضوع الذي هو الجوهر، فاللون مثلًا مقول على السواد والبياض وهي أعراض والزمان موجود في الحركة وهو عرض. وأما الموضوعات التي هي جواهر فأمثلتها ظاهرة وإنها من الموضوعات ما ينتهي إليه الغير من الأعراض. ثم الشيء إذا حصل في شيء حصولاً أولياً لا يتميز منه شيء عن الآخر فيما يحصل لصاحبه منه صفة وهيئة؛ فذلك إما عرض في صاحبه وإما صورة وإنهما أي العرض والصورة يشتركان في أنهما في محل لكن محل أحدهما يسمى مادة، ومحل الآخر يسمى موضوعا، وقد مرَّ الكلام فيهما من قبل.
Then if the major term is predicated of the middle, and the middle of the minor non-essentially, then the major is also predicated of the minor, like 'laughter' of every man, and 'walker' of every laughing free being, so 'walker' of every man. And if the major term exists in the middle, and the middle exists in the minor, then the well-known view is that it is impossible; because an accident is not predicated of an accident. So if the middle is an accident, the major cannot be an accident in the middle, as then it would be an accident in an accident. This is among the things for which neither a definition is necessary nor a demonstration has been established: either that it is not necessary from the definition of 'accident'—for an accident is said to be what exists in a thing with this description, and it has not been shown that that thing is a substance or accident; or that there is no demonstration of it, and these people have not attempted to establish it in their sciences; nor is it in itself something capable of demonstration, for the truth is its opposite. That is because many accidents exist in substances through the mediation of other accidents, like contact, which exists in a body through the mediation of a surface; and it does not follow from its existing in the body through mediation that it exists in the surface, nor that it does not exist in the surface; rather, both statements can be true together, but one is primary and the other secondary, for contact exists in the surface first and then in the body. Similarly, time occurs in motion first and then in the moving body. And what occurs in an accident, both it and the accident are in the subject of the accident, for a thing's being in something gives it a true subject for 'said of' and a subject for 'in', and one does not necessitate that it be the subject which is substance. For example, color is predicated of blackness and whiteness, which are accidents, and time exists in motion, which is an accident. As for subjects that are substances, their examples are obvious, and they are the subjects to which other accidents ultimately terminate. Then when a thing inheres in something primarily, with no distinction of one part from another in the form or quality that accrues to its possessor, it is either an accident in its possessor or a form; and both (accident and form) share that they are in a locus, but the locus of one is called matter and the locus of the other is called subject, and the discussion of them has preceded.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a common logical claim: that an accident cannot be predicated of or inhere in another accident. The author begins by setting up the syllogistic scenario: if the major term is predicated of the middle and the middle of the minor non-essentially, then the major is also predicated of the minor (e.g., 'walking' of every laughing person, so of every man). But if the major exists in the middle and the middle in the minor, the common view says this is impossible because an accident cannot be in an accident. The author then attacks this claim, asserting that it lacks both a proper definition and a demonstration. He argues that the definition of 'accident' as 'what exists in something' does not specify whether that something is substance or accident, so it does not rule out accident-in-accident. Moreover, no proof has been offered. He then provides counterexamples: contact (الملابسة) exists in a body through the surface; it first exists in the surface (an accident of the body) and then in the body. Similarly, time exists in motion (an accident) first, then in the moving body. Thus, accidents can be in accidents. He further clarifies that color is predicated of blackness and whiteness, which are themselves accidents, and time is in motion, also an accident. The passage concludes by distinguishing between the subject of inherence—whether it is a substance or a form/matter—but notes that this discussion is already covered elsewhere. The overall argument is that the claim 'no accident in accident' is not logically necessary and is contradicted by real examples.

Key Takeaway
The claim that an accident cannot inhere in another accident is false; counterexamples from the natural world (e.g., contact in surface, time in motion) demonstrate that accidents can and do inhere in other accidents.

Parsing a Compound Syllogism on Accident and Substance
Author analyzes a compound syllogism on substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse a compound syllogism from an Islamic logical text and evaluate the acceptability of its premises based on intended meaning.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism's validity depends on the precise interpretation of each premise.

In this passage, the author examines a three-premise syllogism used by adversaries to prove that an accident in a composite is a substance. By dissecting each premise, the author shows that logical refutation often hinges on clarifying ambiguous meanings rather than outright rejection or acceptance. This skill is essential for rigorous theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Locate each premise in the Arabic text: first: 'إن العرض في المركب ليس لا كجزء منه'; second: 'وكل ما هو في شيء ليس لا كجزء منه فليس عرضًا فيه'; third: 'وكل ما ليس عرضًا في شيء فهو جوهر'.
The third premise is given but the analysis of it is cut off.
2
Determine the status of the first premise
The author states it is 'من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه' (among that about which there is no dispute). Accept it as conceded.
3
Analyze the second premise's ambiguity
The author says it is 'في حيز المنع' (subject to prevention) and explains two interpretations. If it means everything in a composite in that manner is not an accident at all, it is false because it is an accident as mentioned. If it means it is not in that composite as in a subject, then it is conceded but the conclusion may not follow.
Understanding this ambiguity is key to evaluating the syllogism.
4
Apply the principle of premise evaluation
Realize that in logical analysis, a premise can be accepted in one sense and rejected in another. The key is to clarify the intended meaning before judging the argument.
5
Synthesize the overall argument
Combine the analysis: the first premise is undisputed; the second is ambiguous; the third is likely conceded but the overall conclusion depends on the second. The compound syllogism fails if the second premise is taken in the problematic sense.
This demonstrates the importance of precise language in logical arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم إن العرض في المركب هو فيه ليس لا كجزء منه، وكل ما هو في شيء ليس لا كجزء منه فليس عرضًا فيه، وكل ما ليس عرضًا في شيء فهو جوهر»، فيه قياس مركب يشتمل على ثلاث مقدمات: أما الأولى وهي قوله إن العرض في المركب ليس لا كجزء منه»، فإنها من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه. وأما الثانية وهي قوله وكل ما هو في شيء ليس لا كجزء منه فليس عرضًا فيه»، فإنها في حيز المنع إذا كان المراد منه أن كل ما هو في المركب بهذه الصفة فذلك ليس في نفسه عرضًا وهو فيه؛ إذ هو عرض لما مر . وإن كان المراد منه هو أنه لا يكون فيه على أن ذلك موضوعه وهو فيه في موضوع؛ فذلك مسلم لكنه لا
And their statement that the accident in the composite is in it not as not a part of it, and everything that is in a thing not as not a part of it is not an accident in it, and everything that is not an accident in a thing is a substance. In it is a compound syllogism consisting of three premises: as for the first, which is his statement that the accident in the composite is not as not a part of it, it is among that about which there is no dispute. As for the second, which is his statement that everything that is in a thing not as not a part of it is not an accident in it, it is subject to prevention, if what is meant is that everything that is in the composite in this description is not in itself an accident while being in it, because it is an accident as mentioned. And if what is meant is that it is not in it such that that is its subject while it is in it in a subject, then that is conceded but it...
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of a compound syllogism (قياس مركب) that the opponents use to argue that an accident in a composite is actually a substance. The author identifies three premises. The first premise ('the accident in the composite is not as not a part of it') is accepted without dispute. The second premise is that anything that is in something not as not a part of it is not an accident in it. The author rejects this premise if it means that such a thing is not an accident at all, because something can be an accident while being in a composite as a part in one way. However, if it means that it is not in it as in a subject (i.e., it is not an accident in that composite), then it is conceded. The third premise is cut off, but the analysis shows the importance of carefully distinguishing the intended meaning of each premise.

Key Terms

مقدمات
في حيز المنع

A) completely true

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism must be evaluated premise by premise, and a premise may be rejected or accepted depending on its precise interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following compound syllogism: 'Every created thing is temporally originated; every temporally originated thing has a cause; therefore every created thing has a cause.' Identify each premise and check if they are all conceded. (From kalam example).
?
In a debate on whether knowledge is an accident, an opponent says: 'Knowledge is an accident because it inheres in the soul; everything that inheres in a substance is an accident; therefore knowledge is an accident.' Analyze the second premise: is it true for all interpretations? (fiqh/kalam context).

Refuting the False Dilemma about the Necessary
Author refutes a false dilemma about necessary being possible or not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the false claim that the necessary must be either possible (special) or impossible, using the distinction between general and special possibility.

Core Idea
The necessary is possible generally but not specially – no contradiction arises.

In this passage, the author takes on a mistaken argument that tries to force the necessary into a false dilemma. The arguer claims that the necessary must be either possible or not possible, and each leads to contradiction. The author shows the flaw: the arguer confuses two meanings of 'possible'. Let's walk through the refutation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Extract the false dilemma
The opponent says: the necessary cannot but be either possible (special) or not possible (general). Each case leads to a contradiction. Identify this dilemma in the Arabic.
2
Analyze the first horn
If the necessary is possible in the special sense, then since the special possible can possibly not be, the necessary could possibly not be – contradiction. The author agrees this is absurd.
Special possible admits both existence and non-existence.
3
Analyze the second horn
If the necessary is not possible in the general sense, then it would be impossible – also contradiction. The author rejects this too.
General possible includes the necessary.
4
Identify the resolution
The author points out that 'possible' has two meanings: general (includes necessary) and special (excludes necessary). The necessary is possible in the general sense but not in the special sense, so neither horn applies. The dilemma is false.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الواجب يدخل في الأول منها لا غير، وغير الواجب يدخل في الكل إلّا ولا أن يكون العدم في الحال شرطا للأخص منها. فإن الموجود الذي لا يجب وجوده يدخل في الأول منها والثاني لا غير حينئذ. وبهذا يظهر فساد قول من قال إن الواجب لا يخلو من أن يكون ممكنا أو لا يكون، فإن كان ممكنا والممكن أن يكون ممكنا أن لا يكون فالواجب ممكن أن لا يكون; هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن ممكنا، واللا ممكن ممتنع فالواجب ممتنع; وهذا خلف أيضا; إذ الواجب ممكن بالإمكان العام، وغير ممكن بالإمكان الخاص; فلا يلزم من كونه ممكنا أن لا يكون واجبا، ولا يلزم من عدم كونه ممكنا أن يكون ممتنعا . فإذن قوله إن الواجب لا يخلو إما في المتعين منهما; وإنه لا يتم أولاً في المتعين»، وذلك باطل لما أنه ممكن ، ولا يمكن أيضًا كما مرَّ. فالممكن اسم يطلق على ما بالقوة وعلى ما بالضرورة أيضًا: والأول يصدق فيه ممكن أن يكون وممكن أن لا يكون على خلاف الثاني فإنه لا يصدق فيه ممكن أن لا يكون. وعلى هذا إن ما يسلب عنه الإمكان فلا يلزم أن يكون ممتنعا . فإن الأول وهو الممكن الذي بالقوة يسلب عن الثاني وهو الممكن الذي بالضرورة ولا يلزم منه أنه ممتنع ثم الممكن يطلق على الضروري من حيث إنه عام يعم الضروري وغيره / لا من حيث إنه اسمه بوجه. ثم ههنا بحث آخر وهو أن القوة اسم أخص من الممكن الذي ليس بضروري: فإن الشيء الذي في القوة شرطه أن يكون معدوما، والذي ليس بضروري لا يكون مشروطًا بهذا الشرط فلا يبعد أن يكون موجودًا في الحال أو لا يكون موجودًا. فإن قلت الشيء إذا كان موجودًا في الحال كان واجبا من حيث إنه موجود فكذلك إذا كان معدوما; إذ هو ممتنع من حيث إنه معدوم. فالذي في القوة على ما مر كان ممتنعا لا ممكنا. فنقول: الواجب قد يكون بالذات وهو الدائم الوجود، وقد لا يكون بالذات بل بالغير كالواجب بشرط وقت أو حال. وكذلك الممتنع قد يكون بالذات وهو الدائم العدم، وقد لا يكون بالذات بل بالغير كالممتنع بشرط وقت أو حال أيضًا. وعلى هذا في الممكن من حيث إنه ممكن فإنه قد يكون بالذات وقد
Then the necessary enters into the first only, while the non-necessary enters into all except that non-existence in the present is a condition for the most specific of them. For the existent whose existence is not necessary enters into the first and second only at that time. And by this is shown the corruption of the statement of one who said that the necessary cannot but be either possible or not possible: if it is possible, and [if] the possible can possibly not be, then the necessary can possibly not be — this is a contradiction. And if it is not possible, and the not-possible is impossible, then the necessary is impossible — this is also a contradiction. Because the necessary is possible in the general sense and not possible in the special sense; so it does not follow from its being possible that it is not necessary, nor from its not being possible that it is impossible. So his statement 'the necessary cannot but be one of the two' is invalid; and it does not hold primarily in the two [cases]?] and that is false because it is possible, and it cannot be as well, as previously. So 'possible' is a name applied to what is potential and also to what is necessary: the first admits 'possibly it is and possibly it is not', unlike the second which does not admit 'possibly it is not'. And on this basis, if something is denied possibility, it does not necessarily follow that it is impossible. For the first, which is the possible in potentiality, is denied of the second, which is the possible in necessity, and it does not follow from that that it is impossible. Then 'possible' is applied to the necessary insofar as it is general, encompassing the necessary and other than it, not by way of being its name. Then here is another investigation: that 'potentiality' is a name more specific than the possible that is not necessary: for the thing in potentiality its condition is that it be non-existent, and what is not necessary is not conditioned by this condition, so it is not far that it exists in the present or does not exist. For if you say: 'If something exists in the present, it is necessary insofar as it exists; similarly if it is non-existent, for it is impossible insofar as it is non-existent.' So what is in potentiality according to what preceded was impossible, not possible. So we say: the necessary may be essential, which is perpetual existence, and may not be essential but by another, like the necessary conditional upon a time or state. And similarly the impossible may be essential, which is perpetual non-existence, and may not be essential but by another, like the impossible conditional upon a time or state as well. And on this basis, the possible insofar as it is possible may be essential and may
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a false claim about the relationship between the necessary and the possible, followed by a discussion of potentiality. The author begins by noting that the necessary (الواجب) falls only under the first meaning of 'possible' (general), not the special meaning. The non-necessary falls under all meanings except the most specific, which requires present non-existence. The author then refutes someone who argues that the necessary must be either possible or not possible, and that each alternative leads to contradiction. The refutation shows that the necessary is possible in the general sense but not in the special sense, so no contradiction arises. The argument uses a reductio: if the necessary were possible (in the special sense), then since the special possible can possibly not be, the necessary could possibly not be, which is false. If it were not possible (in the general sense), then it would be impossible, also false. The author then distinguishes between two types of 'possible': potential (ما بالقوة) and necessary (ما بالضرورة). The potential possible admits 'possibly is and possibly is not', unlike the necessary possible which does not admit 'possibly is not'. Importantly, denying possibility from something does not entail impossibility; the potential possible is denied of the necessary possible, yet the necessary is not impossible. The author then introduces a new investigation: 'potentiality' (القوة) is more specific than the non-necessary possible; it requires actual non-existence. The non-necessary possible can exist in the present, but potentiality cannot. An objection is raised: if something exists in the present, it is necessary in that respect; if non-existent, it is impossible. So potentiality would be impossible. The author answers by distinguishing essential vs. accidental necessity/impossibility: the necessary can be essential (always existent) or accidental (conditional on time). Similarly for impossible. The possible can also be essential or accidental. This distinction preserves the possibility of potentiality. The passage underscores the precision required in modal logic, especially in theological discussions of God's attributes and creation.

Key Terms
الواجب (al-wajib): The necessary: that which cannot be otherwise; can be essential (perpetual) or accidental (conditional).
الممكن (al-mumkin): Applied to both the potential (non-necessary) and the necessary (in the general sense). [Note: The twofold application (potential and necessary) is a refinement; typically 'possible' excludes the necessary.]
خلف (khalaf): A contradiction or absurd consequence in an argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the error in the opponent's claim that the necessary must be either possible or not possible?
A) The opponent ignores that the necessary can be both possible and impossible.
B) The opponent fails to distinguish between general and special possibility.
C) The opponent confuses potentiality with necessity.
D) The opponent assumes the necessary is possible in all senses.

Key Takeaway
A proper understanding of modal terms prevents false dilemmas that can lead to theological errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a similar refutation: Someone claims that a created thing must be either necessary or impossible. Using the author's method, show that it is possible (special) and neither necessary nor impossible.
?

Distinguishing Types of Possibility and Potentiality
Author explores meanings of 'possible' and introduces potentiality and modal distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the potential possible (al-quwwa) and the necessary possible, and between essential and accidental necessity/impossibility.

Core Idea
Potentiality requires non-existence; the non-necessary possible can exist; essential vs. accidental modality refines the categories.

After refuting the false dilemma, the author delves deeper into the meanings of 'possible' and introduces the concept of potentiality. He also distinguishes between essential (perpetual) and accidental (conditional) modes of necessity and impossibility. This precision is crucial for theological discussions about God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of 'possible'
The author states that 'possible' applies to both the potential (ما بالقوة) and the necessary (ما بالضرورة). The potential admits 'possibly is and possibly is not', while the necessary does not admit 'possibly is not'.
2
Understand the denial of possibility
Denying possibility of the necessary (i.e., it is not possible in the special sense) does not make it impossible. The potential possible is denied of the necessary possible, but the necessary is not impossible.
3
Define potentiality (al-quwwa)
Potentiality is more specific than the non-necessary possible: its condition is that it be non-existent. Non-necessary possible things can exist in the present, but potentiality cannot.
4
Address the objection
The objector says: if something exists in the present, it is necessary in that respect; if non-existent, it is impossible. So potentiality (non-existent) would be impossible. The author responds by distinguishing essential vs. accidental necessity.
5
Apply the essential/accidental distinction
The necessary can be essential (always existent) or accidental (conditional on time/state). Similarly for impossible and possible. Potentiality is non-existent but not essentially impossible; it is possible in the sense of being contingent on a condition.

Commentary
This passage is a critical refutation of a false claim about the relationship between the necessary and the possible, followed by a discussion of potentiality. The author begins by noting that the necessary (الواجب) falls only under the first meaning of 'possible' (general), not the special meaning. The non-necessary falls under all meanings except the most specific, which requires present non-existence. The author then refutes someone who argues that the necessary must be either possible or not possible, and that each alternative leads to contradiction. The refutation shows that the necessary is possible in the general sense but not in the special sense, so no contradiction arises. The argument uses a reductio: if the necessary were possible (in the special sense), then since the special possible can possibly not be, the necessary could possibly not be, which is false. If it were not possible (in the general sense), then it would be impossible, also false. The author then distinguishes between two types of 'possible': potential (ما بالقوة) and necessary (ما بالضرورة). The potential possible admits 'possibly is and possibly is not', unlike the necessary possible which does not admit 'possibly is not'. Importantly, denying possibility from something does not entail impossibility; the potential possible is denied of the necessary possible, yet the necessary is not impossible. The author then introduces a new investigation: 'potentiality' (القوة) is more specific than the non-necessary possible; it requires actual non-existence. The non-necessary possible can exist in the present, but potentiality cannot. An objection is raised: if something exists in the present, it is necessary in that respect; if non-existent, it is impossible. So potentiality would be impossible. The author answers by distinguishing essential vs. accidental necessity/impossibility: the necessary can be essential (always existent) or accidental (conditional on time). Similarly for impossible. The possible can also be essential or accidental. This distinction preserves the possibility of potentiality. The passage underscores the precision required in modal logic, especially in theological discussions of God's attributes and creation.

Key Terms
القوة (al-quwwa): Potentiality: a mode of existence that is not yet actual, requiring nonexistence.
الممكن (al-mumkin): Applied to both the potential (non-necessary) and the necessary (in the general sense). [Note: The twofold application (potential and necessary) is a refinement; typically 'possible' excludes the necessary.]
بالذات (bi-dhat): Essential: by virtue of itself, perpetual.
بالغير (bi-ghayr): Accidental: by another, conditional upon time or state.

Key Takeaway
Potentiality is a specific kind of possibility that requires non-existence, and modality can be essential or conditional.
