اللوازم فذلك لا يلزم أن يكون في اللزوم على وفق الأصل يمكن أن يكون في الأصل من الجانبين، وفي اللزوم من جانب واحد، وبالعكس سواء كان ذلك اللازم من الموجبات أو من السوالب. ثم الشرطية إذا كانت كلية كما مر من قبل موجبة وسالبة يلزمها من الجزئيات على حسب ما يلزمها من الكليات فإن الموجبة الكلية لا يمكن وجودها إلا وأن تكون الجزئية لازمة لها طردًا وعكسا كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب فـ «ج د» فإنه لا يصدق هذا إلا وأن يصدق قولنا قد يكون إذا كان «أ ب ف ج د وكذلك قولنا قد يكون إذا كان (ج) د ف «أب». وعلى هذا في السالبة الجزئية فإنه يلزم من صدق الموجبة الكلية صدق السالبة الكلية كما مرّ فيصدق إذن ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس (ج) د » ، ولا تصدق هذه السالبة إلا وأن يصدق قولنا ليس كلَّما كان «أب» فليس جد»، فالموجبة الكلية لا تكون من اللوازم إلا وأن تكون الجزئية منها. وكذلك السالبة الكلية فإنها لا تكون من اللوازم إلا وأن تكون الجزئية منها، فاللوازم الجزئية من الموجبات والسوالب أكثر تعددا بالنسبة إلى اللوازم الكلية لما أنه يلزم من صدق الكلية صدق الجزئية ولا ينعكس. ثم الكلية قد تكون مستلزمة للكلية، وقد لا تكون بل تكون مستلزمة للجزئية موجبةً كانت أو سالبة؛ فهذه هي اللازمة الكلية . وأما السالبة الكلية فإنها قد تكون مستلزمةً لجميع ما يكون من اللوازم للموجبة الكلية، وقد لا تكون أما المستلزمة فهي التي لا تكون بدون الموجبة الكلية البتة، ولا الموجبة الكلية بدونها كذلك كما في قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان «أ ب» فليس «ج د»؛ إذ السالبة منهما مستلزمة للموجبات والسوالب اللازمة للموجبة كلية وجزئية. أما الكلية من الموجبات فمثل ما يتركب من المقدم والتالي كما مر وهو قولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د». وكذلك ما يتركب من أحدهما وعين التالي، ومن نقيض أحدهما ونقيض المقدم هلم جرا إلى آخر الموجبات. وبالجملة فكما أن السالبة لازمة للموجبة فكذلك الموجبة
As for the negative implications, they are multiple according to the multiplicity of the affirmatives, because the affirmative is not true from this and that unless the negative from this and the opposite of that is true. So when our statement 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' is true, then our statement 'Absolutely not: if A is B, then C is not D' is true; otherwise one of the two contradictories would entail the other. For the truth of the affirmative does not separate from the truth of some negative at that time; that is impossible. And some claimed that it is not impossible and said: How can it be impossible when there are suppositions where one of the two contradictories cannot separate from the other under that supposition, like the supposition of the conjunction of two opposites? But his claim is false, because the impossible in our case is entailed in reality, not under any supposition. Likewise, if our statement 'Whenever C is not D, A is not B' is true, then our statement 'Absolutely not: if C is not D, then it is not that A is not B' is true; otherwise the entailment would be false. And likewise, if our statement 'Whenever this or that is that' is true, and our statement 'Whenever this or that is not, this is not', then our statement 'Absolutely not: if this or that is, then that is not' is true. And also 'Absolutely not: if this or that is not, then this is not' is true. And so on for all the affirmatives that were mentioned, for the affirmative is not true unless the negative is true. Then, regarding the entailments, it is not necessary that the entailment corresponds to the original in both sides; it may be that in the original it is from both sides, and in the entailment from one side, or vice versa, whether that entailment is from affirmatives or negatives. Then, the conditional, if it is universal as previously mentioned, affirmative or negative, particular conditionals entail it according to what universal conditionals entail from it. For the universal affirmative cannot exist unless the particular is entailed by it directly and conversely, as in our statement 'Whenever A is B, then C is D'; this is not true unless our statement 'It may be that if A is B, then C is D' is true, and also our statement 'It may be that if C is D, then A is B'. And likewise for the particular negative, for from the truth of the universal affirmative the truth of the universal negative follows, as was mentioned; so then 'Absolutely not: if A is B, then C is not D' is true, and this negative is not true unless our statement 'Not every time when A is B, C is not D' is true. So the universal affirmative is not among the entailments unless the particular is among them. Likewise, the universal negative is not among the entailments unless the particular is among them. So the particular entailments, from affirmatives and negatives, are more numerous than the universal entailments, because the truth of the universal entails the truth of the particular, but not vice versa. Then, the universal may entail the universal, or it may not, but rather it entails the particular, whether affirmative or negative. These are the universal entailments. As for the universal negative, it may entail all the entailments of the universal affirmative, or it may not. The one that entails is that which is never without the universal affirmative, and the universal affirmative is never without it, as in our statement 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Absolutely not: if A is B, then C is not D'; for the negative of the two entails the affirmatives and the negatives entailed by the affirmative, universally and particularly. As for the universal of the affirmatives, it is like that which is composed of the antecedent and consequent as mentioned, which is our statement 'Whenever A is B, then C is D'. And likewise what is composed of one of them and the very consequent, and from the contradictory of one and the contradictory of the antecedent, and so on to the end of the affirmatives. In summary, just as the negative is entailed by the affirmative, so too the affirmative
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the logical relationships between affirmative and negative conditional statements, specifically concerning entailments between universal and particular conditionals. The author begins by stating that negative implications are as numerous as affirmative ones. This is because an affirmative conditional, like 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', cannot be true unless the corresponding negative conditional, 'Absolutely not: if A is B, then C is not D', is also true. Otherwise, two contradictories would entail each other, which is impossible. Some might object that in a hypothetical conjunction of opposites, the contradictories might not separate, but the author dismisses this because logical necessity is a matter of reality, not supposition. Then, the author uses examples: if 'Whenever C is not D, A is not B' is true, then 'Absolutely not: if C is not D, then A is B' must also be true. Similarly for conditionals with disjunctive antecedents. The principle is that every affirmative conditional necessarily has a corresponding negative that is also true. Next, the author discusses entailments (lazim) and notes that the direction of entailment may not mirror the original: the original may have two-way entailment while the entailment itself is one-way, or vice versa. Then the focus shifts to universal conditionals. A universal affirmative conditional, such as 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', can only be true if its corresponding particular affirmative conditional, 'It may be that if A is B, then C is D', is also entailed (طردًا وعكسًا). Moreover, the truth of the universal affirmative entails the truth of the universal negative conditional, 'Absolutely not: if A is B, then C is not D', which in turn entails the truth of the particular negative conditional, 'Not every time when A is B, C is not D'. Thus, universal conditionals (both affirmative and negative) cannot be entailments unless their corresponding particular conditionals are also entailments. Consequently, particular entailments are more numerous than universal ones because the universal implies the particular but not vice versa. The author then distinguishes between universal entailments: a universal conditional may entail another universal, or it may only entail a particular. The universal negative is special: it may entail all the entailments of the universal affirmative (as in the pair 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and its negative counterpart), or it may not. The example given shows that the universal negative conditional 'Absolutely not: if A is B, then C is not D' entails both the affirmative and negative entailments of 'Whenever A is B, then C is D'. Finally, the author enumerates various forms of universal affirmatives that can be considered entailments, such as those composed of the antecedent and consequent, or one of them and the consequent, or their contradictories, and concludes that just as the negative is entailed by the affirmative, so too the affirmative is entailed by the negative in the appropriate cases.

Key Terms
لزوم
اللازمة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the direction of entailment between the original conditional and its entailment?
A) The direction must always be the same.
B) The direction may differ; the original may be two-way and the entailment one-way, or vice versa.
C) The direction is always two-way for affirmatives and one-way for negatives.
D) The direction is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The direction of entailment (one-way or two-way) does not have to match between the original conditional and its logical consequence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional: 'Whenever the water is pure (tahir), it is permissible to use for wudu.' Is this entailment one-way or two-way? Now consider the entailment: 'It is not the case that whenever the water is pure, it is not permissible for wudu.' Does the direction of entailment between these two match? Use the author's principle to explain.

Universal Conjunctive Conditionals: Mutual Implication and Conversion
Author illustrates universal conjunctive conditionals with mutual implication and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a given pair of universal conjunctive conditionals are mutually implying and convertible according to the pattern described.

Core Idea
All universal affirmative conjunctive conditionals with the same antecedent are mutually implying and convertible.

In this part of the passage, the author focuses on universal conjunctive conditionals (المتصلات الكلية). He shows that when such conditionals share the same antecedent or consequent, they become mutually implying and convertible. This means that if one is true, all are true, and the relationship holds in both directions.

Guided Path
1
Identify universal quantifier
Look for the phrase 'كلما' (whenever) which indicates a universal conditional. In the passage, the universal conditionals start with 'كلما كان أب فـ ...' (Whenever it is A-B, then ...).
Universal conditionals assert a relationship that holds in every instance.
2
Extract antecedent and consequent
Each universal conditional has an antecedent (e.g., 'أب') and a consequent (e.g., 'ج د'). Note how the author lists several consequents for the same antecedent.
3
Observe the pattern of equivalence
The author states that all affirmatives (الموجبات) among these universals are mutually implying and convertible. For example, if 'Whenever A-B then C-D' is true, then 'Whenever A-B then H-Z' is also true, and vice versa. This holds for all listed pairs.
This indicates that the conditionals are logically equivalent when they share the same antecedent.
4
Apply the reversal
The author also provides reversed examples where the consequent is fixed (ج د) and the antecedents vary (أب, هز, ب ج, etc.). The same mutual implication and conversion hold.
The reversal shows that the property holds symmetrically.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الثالث من المباحث فذلك في بيان ما يكون من الشرطيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة وما تقدم ذكره من الضوابط فيها، فذلك من جملة ما يحكى عنها متصلة كانت أو منفصلة بأنها قد تكون متلازمة ومتعاكسة موجبة كانت أو سالبة كلية كانت أو جزئية. أما المتصلات فإنها لا يخلو من أن تكون متوافقة في المقدم والتالي، أو لا تكون بل تكون متواقفة في المقدم دون التالي أو في التالي دون المقدم والمتخالفة في الصورتين إذا كانت متلازمة ومتعاكسة كانت المتصلات كلها مستلزمة ومتعاكسة على هذا المثال: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «هز» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «هز» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب ج» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب ج» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «به» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «به» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب ز» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب ز» وعلى هذا إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما يقال: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «هز» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «هـ ز» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب ج» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «ب د» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب د» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «ب ز» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب ز» فليس «ج د» فالموجبات كلها متلازمة ومتعاكسة، والسالبات كذلك، وأما في الجزئيات فيقال: قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس «هز» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ب ج» كلما كان «أب» فليس «ب ج» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ب د» ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس «ب د» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ب ز» ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس «ب ز» وعلى هذا إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما يقال: قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فـ «ج د» ليس كلما كان «هـ ز» فليس «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «ب د» فـ «ج د» ليس كلما كان «ب د» فليس «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «ب ز» فـ «ج د» ليس كلما كان «ب ز» فليس «ج د» فالموجبات من هذه الجزئيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة والسالبات كذلك.
As for the third investigation, it is an explanation of what among conditionals are mutually implying and convertible, and the previously mentioned controls concerning them. That is part of what is related about them, whether they are conjunctive or disjunctive, that they may be mutually implying and convertible, whether affirmative or negative, universal or particular. As for conjunctive conditionals, they are not free from being consistent in antecedent and consequent, or not, but rather being consistent in the antecedent without the consequent, or in the consequent without the antecedent, and differing in both forms. When they are mutually implying and convertible, all conjunctive conditionals are mutually implying and convertible according to this example: Whenever it is that A-B, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that A-B, then H-Z; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not H-Z; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-J; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-J; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-D; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-D; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-H; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-H; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-Z; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-Z. And likewise if the matter is reversed, as is said: Whenever it is that A-B, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not C-D; it is not at all that if it is H-Z, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that H-Z, then C-D; Whenever it is that B-J, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is B-J, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that B-D, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is B-D, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that B-Z, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is B-Z, it is not C-D. So all affirmatives are mutually implying and convertible, and likewise the negatives. As for particulars, it is said: It may be that if it is A-B, then C-D; Whenever it is A-B, then C-D; It may be that if it is A-B, then H-Z; It is not that whenever it is A-B, it is not H-Z; It may be that if it is A-B, then B-J; Whenever it is A-B, it is not B-J; It may be that if it is A-B, then B-D; It is not that whenever it is A-B, it is not B-D; It may be that if it is A-B, then B-Z; It is not that whenever it is A-B, it is not B-Z. And likewise if the matter is reversed, as is said: It may be that if it is A-B, then C-D; Whenever it is A-B, then C-D; It may be that if it is H-Z, then C-D; It is not that whenever it is H-Z, it is not C-D; It may be that if it is B-J, then C-D; Whenever it is B-J, then C-D; It may be that if it is B-D, then C-D; It is not that whenever it is B-D, it is not C-D; It may be that if it is B-Z, then C-D; It is not that whenever it is B-Z, it is not C-D. So the affirmatives among these particulars are mutually implying and convertible, and likewise the negatives.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mutual implication and conversion (تلازم وتعاكس) for conditional propositions (شرطيات). The author first distinguishes between universal and particular conditionals, and between affirmative and negative. Then, using symbolic examples (with letters like أب, ج د, etc.), he illustrates patterns for universal conjunctive conditionals (متصلات). The key point is that for universal conditionals, the affirmatives among them are mutually implying and convertible as a whole, and similarly for negatives. For particular conditionals, the same holds but with a weaker form—some are 'it may be that' rather than 'whenever'. The examples show pairs of conditionals that are equivalent (متلازمة) and convertible (متعاكسة). In the universal case, the conditionals are all strongly biconditional; in the particular case, they are only related in a limited way. This is important for understanding how conditional propositions relate to each other in logical deduction.

Key Terms
الشرطيات (al-sharṭiyyāt): Conditional propositions, i.e., propositions that include a conditional connective (if-then or either-or). In this passage, the focus is on conjunctive (متصلة) conditionals. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
متلازمة (mutalāzima): Mutually implying: two conditionals are such that each implies the other (equivalent). [Note: In modern logic, 'mutually implying' is usually called 'logical equivalence'. This term is used in classical Arabic logic for a similar concept.]
متعاكسة (muta‘ākisa): Convertible: two conditionals are such that the antecedent and consequent can be swapped (or the conditional is convertible to another). Here it seems to mean that the relations hold in both directions (i.e., the conditionals are also mutually convertible). [Note: In syllogistic, 'aks' refers to conversion of categorical propositions; here it is applied to conditionals.]
متصلات (muttasilāt): Conjunctive conditionals, i.e., if-then propositions (e.g., 'if A then B'). [Note: This term is standard for conditional propositions in Arabic logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are not mutually implying.
B) They are mutually implying but not convertible.
C) They are mutually implying and convertible.
D) They are neither mutually implying nor convertible.

Key Takeaway
Universal affirmative conjunctive conditionals with the same antecedent are logically equivalent; if one is true, all are true.

Particular Conjunctive Conditionals: Mutual Implication and Conversion
Author illustrates particular conjunctive conditionals with mutual implication and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the pattern of mutual implication and conversion for particular conjunctive conditionals from the universal pattern.

Core Idea
In particular conditionals, mutual implication and conversion hold only among some pairs, with a mix of universal and particular quantifiers.

After discussing universal conditionals, the author turns to particular conjunctive conditionals. These are introduced by 'قد يكون' (it may be that) and sometimes mixed with 'كلما' (whenever). The pattern is similar but not as strong: some conditionals are universal, some particular, and the mutual implication and conversion apply to the affirmatives and negatives within this set.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quantifiers
For particular conditionals, the author uses 'قد يكون' (it may be that) and sometimes 'كلما' (whenever). Note which conditionals have 'كلما' (universal) and which have 'قد يكون' (particular).
In the passage, some conditionals are expressed as 'قد يكون إذا كان أب فـ ج د' (It may be that if A-B then C-D) and next line 'كلما كان أب فـ ج د' (Whenever A-B then C-D).
2
Observe the combination
The author pairs a particular conditional with a universal one. For example, 'قد يكون إذا كان أب فـ ج د' and 'كلما كان أب فـ ج د' appear together. This indicates that the particular is implied by the universal, but not necessarily vice versa? Actually the passage says they are mutually implying and convertible in the set of affirmatives.
3
Extract the pattern for affirmatives
The author concludes: 'فالموجبات من هذه الجزئيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة' meaning the affirmatives among these particulars are mutually implying and convertible. This holds for the conditionals listed, which mix universal and particular quantifiers.
4
Compare with universal case
In the universal case, all affirmatives were universally quantified. Here, some are particular. Yet they still form an equivalence class. The difference is that the particular conditionals are weaker (possible rather than necessary).

وأما الثالث من المباحث فذلك في بيان ما يكون من الشرطيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة وما تقدم ذكره من الضوابط فيها، فذلك من جملة ما يحكى عنها متصلة كانت أو منفصلة بأنها قد تكون متلازمة ومتعاكسة موجبة كانت أو سالبة كلية كانت أو جزئية. أما المتصلات فإنها لا يخلو من أن تكون متوافقة في المقدم والتالي، أو لا تكون بل تكون متواقفة في المقدم دون التالي أو في التالي دون المقدم والمتخالفة في الصورتين إذا كانت متلازمة ومتعاكسة كانت المتصلات كلها مستلزمة ومتعاكسة على هذا المثال: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «هز» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «هز» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب ج» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب ج» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «به» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «به» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ب ز» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ب ز» وعلى هذا إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما يقال: كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «أب» فليس «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «هز» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «هـ ز» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب ج» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «ب د» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب د» فليس «ج د» كلما كان «ب ز» فـ «ج د» ليس البتة إذا كان «ب ز» فليس «ج د» فالموجبات كلها متلازمة ومتعاكسة، والسالبات كذلك، وأما في الجزئيات فيقال: قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «هز» ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس «هز» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ب ج» كلما كان «أب» فليس «ب ج» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ب د» ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس «ب د» قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ب ز» ليس كلما كان «أب» فليس «ب ز» وعلى هذا إذا كان الأمر على العكس كما يقال: قد يكون إذا كان «أب» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «هـ ز» فـ «ج د» ليس كلما كان «هـ ز» فليس «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» كلما كان «ب ج» فـ «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «ب د» فـ «ج د» ليس كلما كان «ب د» فليس «ج د» قد يكون إذا كان «ب ز» فـ «ج د» ليس كلما كان «ب ز» فليس «ج د» فالموجبات من هذه الجزئيات متلازمة ومتعاكسة والسالبات كذلك.
As for the third investigation, it is an explanation of what among conditionals are mutually implying and convertible, and the previously mentioned controls concerning them. That is part of what is related about them, whether they are conjunctive or disjunctive, that they may be mutually implying and convertible, whether affirmative or negative, universal or particular. As for conjunctive conditionals, they are not free from being consistent in antecedent and consequent, or not, but rather being consistent in the antecedent without the consequent, or in the consequent without the antecedent, and differing in both forms. When they are mutually implying and convertible, all conjunctive conditionals are mutually implying and convertible according to this example: Whenever it is that A-B, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that A-B, then H-Z; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not H-Z; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-J; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-J; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-D; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-D; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-H; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-H; Whenever it is that A-B, then B-Z; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not B-Z. And likewise if the matter is reversed, as is said: Whenever it is that A-B, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is A-B, it is not C-D; it is not at all that if it is H-Z, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that H-Z, then C-D; Whenever it is that B-J, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is B-J, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that B-D, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is B-D, it is not C-D; Whenever it is that B-Z, then C-D; it is not at all that if it is B-Z, it is not C-D. So all affirmatives are mutually implying and convertible, and likewise the negatives. As for particulars, it is said: It may be that if it is A-B, then C-D; Whenever it is A-B, then C-D; It may be that if it is A-B, then H-Z; It is not that whenever it is A-B, it is not H-Z; It may be that if it is A-B, then B-J; Whenever it is A-B, it is not B-J; It may be that if it is A-B, then B-D; It is not that whenever it is A-B, it is not B-D; It may be that if it is A-B, then B-Z; It is not that whenever it is A-B, it is not B-Z. And likewise if the matter is reversed, as is said: It may be that if it is A-B, then C-D; Whenever it is A-B, then C-D; It may be that if it is H-Z, then C-D; It is not that whenever it is H-Z, it is not C-D; It may be that if it is B-J, then C-D; Whenever it is B-J, then C-D; It may be that if it is B-D, then C-D; It is not that whenever it is B-D, it is not C-D; It may be that if it is B-Z, then C-D; It is not that whenever it is B-Z, it is not C-D. So the affirmatives among these particulars are mutually implying and convertible, and likewise the negatives.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mutual implication and conversion (تلازم وتعاكس) for conditional propositions (شرطيات). The author first distinguishes between universal and particular conditionals, and between affirmative and negative. Then, using symbolic examples (with letters like أب, ج د, etc.), he illustrates patterns for universal conjunctive conditionals (متصلات). The key point is that for universal conditionals, the affirmatives among them are mutually implying and convertible as a whole, and similarly for negatives. For particular conditionals, the same holds but with a weaker form—some are 'it may be that' rather than 'whenever'. The examples show pairs of conditionals that are equivalent (متلازمة) and convertible (متعاكسة). In the universal case, the conditionals are all strongly biconditional; in the particular case, they are only related in a limited way. This is important for understanding how conditional propositions relate to each other in logical deduction.

Key Terms
الشرطيات (al-sharṭiyyāt): Conditional propositions, i.e., propositions that include a conditional connective (if-then or either-or). In this passage, the focus is on conjunctive (متصلة) conditionals. [Note: No significant shift; this term is stable across the tradition.]
متلازمة (mutalāzima): Mutually implying: two conditionals are such that each implies the other (equivalent). [Note: In modern logic, 'mutually implying' is usually called 'logical equivalence'. This term is used in classical Arabic logic for a similar concept.]
متعاكسة (muta‘ākisa): Convertible: two conditionals are such that the antecedent and consequent can be swapped (or the conditional is convertible to another). Here it seems to mean that the relations hold in both directions (i.e., the conditionals are also mutually convertible). [Note: In syllogistic, 'aks' refers to conversion of categorical propositions; here it is applied to conditionals.]
متصلات (muttasilāt): Conjunctive conditionals, i.e., if-then propositions (e.g., 'if A then B'). [Note: This term is standard for conditional propositions in Arabic logic.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is true about the affirmatives among the particular conjunctive conditionals listed?
A) They are not mutually implying.
B) They are mutually implying but not convertible.
C) They are mutually implying and convertible.
D) They are neither mutually implying nor convertible.

Key Takeaway
Even in particular conditionals, affirmatives can be mutually implying and convertible, though the quantifiers may mix universal and particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
advanced
5 min
Logical Implications of Exclusive Disjunctions in Islamic Logic
Author details logical implications of true exclusive disjunctives for conditionals and disjunctives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the necessary conditional statements from a true exclusive disjunction and distinguish the differing implications between its subtypes (man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw).

Core Idea
From one exclusive disjunction, multiple conditional implications cascade.

This passage from the Islamic logical tradition explores the rich consequences of exclusive disjunctions. The author demonstrates that a simple statement like 'Every A is either B or C' yields not only four basic conditionals but also further implications involving terms that necessitate or are necessitated by the disjuncts. Understanding these entailments is essential for rigorous argumentation in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Define the exclusive disjunction
A true exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam' wal khuluw) is true only when exactly one disjunct is true. This property drives all subsequent implications.
2
Derive the four basic conditionals
From 'Every A is either B or C' (exclusive), the following conditionals necessarily follow: whenever it is B it is not C, whenever it is C it is not B, whenever it is not B it is C, and whenever it is not C it is B.
These are immediate inferences from the disjunction's truth conditions.
3
Track extended implications
The author shows that implications multiply: being B implies not only not C but also the non-existence of anything that necessitates C. Similarly, not being B implies the existence of consequences of C, etc.
This expands the logical network beyond the simple disjuncts.
4
Compare the subtypes
For man'at al-jam' (prevents conjunction only), the implication from negation of one disjunct to affirmation of the other does NOT hold. For man'at al-khuluw (prevents vacancy only), the implication from affirmation of one disjunct to negation of the other does NOT hold.
Subtypes limit which conditionals follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : في الأول والثاني من المباحث في اللوازم من المتصلات والمنفصلات.
وأما المنفصلات فالحقيقية منها وهي المانعة من الجمع والخلو مستلزمة للمتصلات والمنفصلات موجبة وسالبة وكلية وجزئية كذلك لما مرّ من قبل أن الشيء إذا كان مستلزما للشيء كان معاندا لما يناقضه، وبالعكس. أما المتصلات من اللوازم فبينة؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «أ» إما «ب» وإما «ج»، فقد صدق قولنا كلما كان «ب» فليس «ج»، كلما كان «ج» فليس «ب»، كلما لم يكن «ب» فهو «ج»، كلما لم يكن «ج» فهو «ب». وكما أن كونه أ ب مستلزم لعدم كونه «ج»، فكذلك مستلزم لعدم ما يكون مستلزما لـ«ج». وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون مستلزما له مساويًا كان ذلك المستلزم، أو أخص منه. وكذلك كونه ج لعدم ب ولعدم ما يكون مستلزما له، وإنه متعدد أيضًا حسب تعدد ما يكون مستلزما له. وأيضًا كما أن عدم (ب) مستلزم لـ «ج»، فكذلك مستلزم لوجود ما يكون من لوازمه، وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم. وكذلك عدم «ج» لوجود (ب) ولوجود ما يكون من لوازمه؛ وإنه متعدد أيضًا حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم.
وعلى هذا في المانعة من الجمع فإنه إذا صدق قولنا دائما كل «ج» إما (ب) وإما «د»، صدق قولنا إنه إذا كان «ب» فليس «د»، وإذا كان «د» فليس «ب». وكما أن كونه «ب» مستلزم لعدم كونه «د»، فكذلك لعدم ما يكون مستلزما له؛ وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون مستلزما له. وكذلك كونه د لعدم كونه (ب) ولعدم ما يكون مستلزما له؛ وإنه متعدد أيضًا حسب تعدد ما يكون مستلزما له، ولا يلزم من عدم كونه (ب) لا كونه د ولا عدم كونه كذلك. وكذلك لا يلزم من عدم كونه ((د) لا كونه (ب) ولا عدم كونه أيضًا.
وكذلك في المانعة من الخلو فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل «د» إما «هـ» وإما ليس «ز»، صدق قولنا إنه إذا لم يكن (هـ) فهو ليس ز)، وإذا لم يكن ليس ز فهو (هـ). وكما أن عدم كونه (هـ) مستلزم لوجود ذلك وهو ليس «ز»، فكذلك لوجود ما يكون من لوازمه؛ وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم. وكذلك عدم كونه ليس ((د)) لوجود (هـ)؛ ولوجود ما يكون من لوازمه وإنه متعدد حسب تعدد ما يكون من اللوازم أيضًا، ولا يلزم من كونه (هـ) لا كونه ز ولا ليس كونه ز. وكذلك لا يلزم من كونه ليس «ز» لا كونه «ه» ولا ليس كونه «ه» كذلك.
ثم اللزوم في جميع هذه المتّصلات من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان بل يعرف بالتأمل، وإن عدم اللزوم مما يستلزم عدم العناد في الأصل، وهذا ظاهر.
Section: On the first and second investigations regarding implications of conditionals and disjunctives. As for disjunctives, the true ones—which are the exclusive disjunctives (preventing both conjunction and vacancy)—necessitate conditionals and disjunctives, affirmative and negative, universal and particular, similarly, according to what has passed: because a thing, if it necessitates something, is opposite to what contradicts it, and vice versa. As for the conditionals from the implications, they are evident; that is because if our statement is always true: 'Every A is either B or C', then our statement is true: 'Whenever it is B, it is not C', 'Whenever it is C, it is not B', 'Whenever it is not B, it is C', 'Whenever it is not C, it is B'. And just as its being A B necessitates its not being C, so too it necessitates the non-existence of what necessitates C. And it is multiple according to the multiplicity of what necessitates it, whether that necessitator is equal or more specific. And similarly, its being C necessitates its not being B and the non-existence of what necessitates B, and it is also multiple according to the multiplicity of what necessitates it. Also, just as the non-existence of B necessitates C, so too it necessitates the existence of its necessary consequences, and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of those consequences. And similarly, the non-existence of C necessitates the existence of B and the existence of its necessary consequences; and it is also multiple according to the multiplicity of those consequences. And accordingly, in the disjunctive that prevents conjunction: if our statement is always true: 'Every C is either B or D', our statement is true: 'If it is B, it is not D', and 'If it is D, it is not B'. And just as its being B necessitates its not being D, so too it necessitates the non-existence of what necessitates D; and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of what necessitates it. And similarly, its being D necessitates its not being B and the non-existence of what necessitates B; and it is also multiple according to the multiplicity of what necessitates it, and it does not follow from its not being B either its being D or its not being so. And similarly, it does not follow from its not being D either its being B or its not being so. And likewise, in the disjunctive that prevents vacancy: if our statement is always true: 'Every D is either H or not Z', our statement is true: 'If it is not H, it is not Z', and 'If it is not not Z, it is H'. And just as the non-existence of H necessitates the existence of that which is not Z, so too it necessitates the existence of its necessary consequences; and it is multiple according to the multiplicity of those consequences. And similarly, the non-existence of its not being D necessitates the existence of H; and the existence of its necessary consequences, and it is also multiple according to the multiplicity of those consequences. And it does not follow from its being H either its being Z or its not being Z. And similarly, it does not follow from its being not Z either its being H or its not being H. Then the implication in all these conditionals is among things that need no explanation but are known by reflection, and the absence of implication necessitates the absence of opposition in principle, and this is evident.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exploration of the logical implications arising from true exclusive disjunctives (man'at al-jam' wal khuluw). The author begins by stating that such disjunctives necessarily imply both conditionals and disjunctives across all quantifications and qualities. The reasoning is that if one thing necessitates another, then it is opposite to what contradicts that thing. The author then demonstrates this with examples: if the statement 'Every A is either B or C' is always true (i.e., a universal exclusive disjunction), then four conditional statements necessarily follow: (1) whenever it is B, it is not C; (2) whenever it is C, it is not B; (3) whenever it is not B, it is C; and (4) whenever it is not C, it is B. The author then explains that the implication extends beyond simple terms: being B also implies the non-existence of anything that necessitates C, and this can be multiple depending on what necessitates C. Similarly for being C, not being B, and not being C. The author then distinguishes between the two subtypes of exclusive disjunctives: the one that prevents conjunction (man'at al-jam') and the one that prevents vacancy (man'at al-khuluw). For the former, if 'Every C is either B or D' is always true, then the conditionals 'If B then not D' and 'If D then not B' follow. For the latter, if 'Every D is either H or not Z' is always true, then the conditionals 'If not H then not Z' and 'If not not Z then H' follow. In each case, the author notes the multiplicity of implications and explicitly states what does not follow (e.g., from not being B, neither being D nor not being D necessarily follows in the preventing-conjunction type). The passage concludes by noting that these implications are self-evident upon reflection, and that the absence of implication would indicate the absence of opposition in the original disjunction.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية (al-munfasilah al-haqiqiyyah): The true disjunctive, also called exclusive disjunction (man'at al-jam' wal khuluw), which is true only when exactly one of the two disjuncts is true. [Note: No significant shift; stable across the tradition.]
المانعة من الجمع والخلو (al-mani'ah min al-jam' wal khuluw): The exclusive disjunction that prevents both the conjunction of both disjuncts and the vacancy (absence) of both; i.e., exactly one true. [Note: No significant shift; standard term in Arabic logic.]
المانعة من الجمع (al-mani'ah min al-jam'): A disjunction that prevents both being true together, but allows both to be false. In the text, it is treated as a subtype of exclusive disjunction. [Note: In later texts, man'at al-jam' often refers to exclusive disjunction in the sense of 'at most one true'; here it is a subtype.]
المانعة من الخلو (al-mani'ah min al-khuluw): A disjunction that prevents both being false together, but allows both to be true (i.e., at least one true). The text uses it as the other subtype. [Note: In later texts, man'at al-khuluw often refers to exclusive disjunction in the sense of 'at least one true'; here it is a subtype.]
اللزوم (al-luzum): Necessary implication or entailment between propositions; if p then q is necessary. [Note: No significant shift; stable term for logical consequence.]
العناد (al-'inad): Opposition or contradiction; a relationship where two propositions cannot both be true together (in context of man'at al-jam'). [Note: Used here in a technical sense; in other contexts it may mean stubbornness.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If 'Every substance is either accident or body' is a true exclusive disjunction, which of the following conditionals necessarily follows?
A) Whenever it is an accident, it is a body.
B) Whenever it is not an accident, it is a body.
C) Whenever it is not a body, it is not an accident.
D) Whenever it is an accident, it is not not a body.

Key Takeaway
A true exclusive disjunction entails multiple conditional implications, but the subtypes (man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw) restrict which implications hold from negation or affirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
